{"uri": "2024-07-417754834", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "17:29:54", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T17:29:54Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T17:06:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9529411792755127, "url": "https://omaha.com/wimbledon-injuries-djokovic-de-minaur-collins-keys/article_8ef68259-3a16-5b8c-8434-0d9ae5b2af19.html", "title": "Injuries are adding up at Wimbledon and determining the outcomes of matches", "body": "Novak Djokovic of Serbia falls during Monday's fourth round match against Holger Rune of Denmark at Wimbledon in London.\n\nHOWARD FENDRICH and KEN MAGUIRE Associated Press\n\nLONDON -- There's no single explanation, of course, for all of the injuries to players in the latter stages at Wimbledon this year. This much is certain: The timing could hardly be worse.\n\nThe man Novak Djokovic was supposed to face on Wednesday, Alex de Minaur, withdrew hours before their scheduled quarterfinal because he jarred his hip at the end of a victory two days earlier.\n\n\"I'm devastated,\" de Minaur said. \"The problem with me going out and playing is that one stretch, one slide, one anything, can make this injury (recovery) go from three to six weeks to four months. It's too much to risk.\"\n\nTaylor Fritz's fourth-round opponent, Alexander Zverev, slipped on an unworn patch of green grass in his previous match. That caused a bone bruise -- and maybe worse -- that Zverev complained left him on \"one leg\" in his loss to the American at what the two-time major finalist characterized as a wide open opportunity to grab a first Grand Slam title.\n\nDanielle Collins' last Wimbledon appearance before retirement ended with tape wrapped around her hamstring, the work of a trainer during the American's fourth-round loss to 2021 French Open champion Barbora Krejcikova. Collins chalked it up to failing to \"think about every little step that you take.\"\n\n\"There's been a ton of injuries on the grass. Left and right, it seems like people are going down. I am, I guess, frustrated that I feel like I was focusing on my tactics and kind of what I needed to do to play at a high level. Usually, I feel like, on other surfaces you're not having to think so critically about your movement,\" Collins said. \"The one second I take my mind off of it, not think about every little thing I'm doing with my footwork, it ends up happening.\"\n\nThe falls keep happening. The injuries are adding up.\n\n\"It's unfortunate, obviously,\" de Minaur said. \"You never want to see this.\"\n\nHe called his mishap \"more of a freak injury,\" related to the \"excessive amount of force\" used to slide on grass.\n\nMadison Keys, the 2017 U.S. Open runner-up, was in tears when she stopped because of a hurt leg at 5-all in the third set of a Week 2 match against Jasmine Paolini, who reached Thursday's semifinals.\n\nEmma Raducanu, who won the U.S. Open three years ago, withdrew from mixed doubles -- which was supposed to be Andy Murray's last event at Wimbledon -- because of a sore wrist, then needed a medical timeout later that day after falling in the third set of a singles loss.\n\nNo. 17 Anna Kalinskaya cited a bad wrist when she quit in her fourth-round match against 2022 champion Elena Rybakina. No. 10 Grigor Dimitrov retired from his fourth-round match against Daniil Medvedev with a leg problem.\n\n\"It's normal for the second week at Wimbledon to be feeling niggling things on your muscles, because it's tough -- the grass, getting down low, coming into the net. It's more on the muscles than the joints on the grass,\" 2003 Wimbledon finalist Mark Philippoussis said. \"So I'm sure lots of people will be feeling it now with little things here and there.\"\n\nCount Djokovic among those who think part of the issue is that all of the rain during the tournament -- so much that the mixed doubles final was shifted from Thursday to Sunday, and play began a half-hour earlier than usual on most courts Wednesday -- has made the grass more slick and the footing less sure.\n\nSo has shutting the retractable roofs at Centre Court and No. 1 Court, the only two arenas with that luxury during wet weather.\n\n\"Once you close the roof, you know that the grass is going to be more slippery. So there's more chances that players will fall. Unfortunately, some of the falls have caused some of the players to withdraw,\" Djokovic said.\n\n\"It's part of this surface. You can't really change that,\" added the seven-time champion at the All England Club. \"I mean, it's grass. It's a live surface, and it reacts to different conditions.\"\n\nThe pattern began at grass tourneys that preceded Wimbledon.\n\nMarketa Vondrousova retired from a match in Berlin after hurting her right leg there. When she showed up at Wimbledon, she became the first defending champion in 30 years to lose in the first round and acknowledged: \"I was a bit scared because of my leg.\"\n\nThe woman who beat her last week, Jessica Bouzas Maneiro, stopped at Wimbledon because of a back issue in the third round against Krejcikova.\n\nFrances Tiafoe pulled out of the Queen's Club tournament before Wimbledon after spraining a ligament in his right knee when he took a tumble. Tiafoe played at the All England Club with a black sleeve over his knee and made it to the third round before losing to defending champion Carlos Alcaraz.\n\nDjokovic tore the meniscus in his right knee during a match at the French Open, had surgery and returned to competition less than a month later. Ironically, he thinks it's possible that might have helped him stay upright this fortnight.\n\nThat's because, years ago, Djokovic was one of the first players to regularly slide on grass the way they do on clay. He has cut down on those movements this time at Wimbledon, being extra careful to avoid risking falls.\n\n\"It's probably part of my, I guess, different kind of movement on the court that I've been really experimenting with because of the cautiousness -- because of the knee and everything that was happening prior to the tournament,\" Djokovic explained. \"The first couple rounds, I was still not maybe willing to go (for difficult) balls and slide and make splits.\"\n\nOther theories include: More and more baseline play on grass, and less serving-and-volleying, creates longer points and extra running, which translate into a greater likelihood of slips; less comfort on grass because players tend to grow up practicing and competing on clay or hard courts; and a brief grass portion of the schedule that doesn't allow for accumulating a lot of experience on the turf.\n\nThen there's the general wear-and-tear of a season.\n\n\"Listen, tennis is a very physical sport at the moment. For sure, the rallies are longer. Matches. Scheduling. Finishing late,\" 2006 Australian Open finalist Marcos Baghdatis said. \"It's very demanding on the body. ... A lot of things are changing that (contribute) to players getting injured.\"", "source": {"uri": "omaha.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "Omaha.com"}, "authors": [{"uri": You can't really change that,\" added the seven-time champion at the All England Club. \"I mean, it's grass. It's a live surface, and it reacts to different conditions.\"\n\nThe pattern began at grass tourneys that preceded Wimbledon.\n\nMarketa Vondrousova retired from a match in Berlin after hurting her right leg there. When she showed up at Wimbledon, she became the first defending champion in 30 years to lose in the first round and acknowledged: \"I was a bit scared because of my leg.\"\n\nThe woman who beat her last week, Jessica Bouzas Maneiro, stopped at Wimbledon because of a back issue in the third round against Krejcikova.\n\nFrances Tiafoe pulled out of the Queen's Club tournament before Wimbledon after spraining a ligament in his right knee when he took a tumble. Tiafoe played at the All England Club with a black sleeve over his knee and made it to the third round before losing to defending champion Carlos Alcaraz.\n\nSpain's Carlos Alcaraz, left, looks over at Francis Tiafoe of the United States who fell during their third round match at the Wimbledon tennis championships in London, Friday, July 5, 2024. (Alberto Pezzali/AP)Djokovic tore the meniscus in his right knee during a match at the French Open, had surgery and returned to competition less than a month later. Ironically, he thinks it's possible that might have helped him stay upright this fortnight.\n\nThat's because, years ago, Djokovic was one of the first players to regularly slide on grass the way they do on clay. He has cut down on those movements this time at Wimbledon, being extra careful to avoid risking falls.\n\n\"It's probably part of my, I guess, different kind of movement on the court that I've been really experimenting with because of the cautiousness -- because of the knee and everything that was happening prior to the tournament,\" Djokovic explained. \"The first couple rounds, I was still not maybe willing to go (for difficult) balls and slide and make splits.\"\n\nOther theories include: More and more baseline play on grass, and less serving-and-volleying, creates longer points and extra running, which translate into a greater likelihood of slips; less comfort on grass because players tend to grow up practicing and competing on clay or hard courts; and a brief grass portion of the schedule that doesn't allow for accumulating a lot of experience on the turf.\n\nThen there's the general wear-and-tear of a season.\n\n\"Listen, tennis is a very physical sport at the moment. For sure, the rallies are longer. Matches. Scheduling. Finishing late,\" 2006 Australian Open finalist Marcos Baghdatis said. \"It's very demanding on the body. ... Hours before their scheduled quarterfinal, though, de Minaur withdrew because of a hip injury sustained at the end of a victory two days earlier.\n\nDe Minaur had tried to remain optimistic, and he practiced Wednesday morning before deciding he wouldn't be able to take the competition court.\n\n\"I'm devastated,\" de Minaur said. \"The problem with me going out and playing is that one stretch, one slide, one anything can make this injury go from three to six weeks (of recovery) to four months. It's too much to risk.\"\n\nTaylor Fritz's fourth-round opponent on Wednesday, Alexander Zverev, slipped on an unworn patch of green grass in his previous match. That caused a bone bruise -- and maybe worse -- that Zverev complained left him on \"one leg\" in his loss to Fritz. Zverev, a two-time major finalist, was frustrated by the nature of the end of a run he had characterized as a wide-open opportunity to grab his first Grand Slam title.\n\nDanielle Collins' last Wimbledon appearance before retirement ended with tape wrapped around her hamstring, the work of a trainer during the 30-year-old American's fourth-round loss to 2021 French Open champion Barbora Krejcikova. Collins chalked it up to failing to \"think about every little step that you take.\"\n\n\"There's been a ton of injuries on the grass. Left and right, it seems like people are going down,\" Collins said. \"I am, I guess, frustrated that I feel like I was focusing on my tactics and kind of what I needed to do to play at a high level. Usually, I feel like, on other surfaces you're not having to think so critically about your movement.\n\n\"The one second I take my mind off of it, not think about every little thing I'm doing with my footwork, it ends up happening.\"\n\nAP photo by Mosa'ab Elshamy / Novak Djokovic falls during his fourth-round match against Holger Rune on Monday at Wimbledon. A knee injury sustained at the French Open led to Djokovic withdrawing from the clay-court major tournament and having surgery, but he said that has led to him being more careful about his footing on the grass of the All England Club.\n\nOW, THAT HURTS\n\nThe falls keep happening. The injuries are adding up.\n\n\"It's unfortunate, obviously. You never want to see this,\" said de Minaur, who called his mishap \"more of a freak injury,\" related to the \"excessive amount of force\" used to slide on grass.\n\nMadison Keys, the 2017 U.S. Open runner-up, was in tears earlier in the week when she stopped because of a leg injury at 5-all in the third set of a fourth-round match against Jasmine Paolini, who reached Thursday's semifinals.\n\nEmma Raducanu, who won the U.S. Open women's singles title three years ago, withdrew from mixed doubles -- which was supposed to be fellow British player Andy Murray's last event at Wimbledon -- because of a sore wrist, then needed a medical timeout later that day after falling in the third set of a singles loss.\n\nNo. 17 Anna Kalinskaya cited a bad wrist when she quit in her fourth-round match against 2022 champion Elena Rybakina. No. 10 Grigor Dimitrov retired from his fourth-round match against No. 5 Daniil Medvedev with a leg problem.\n\n\"It's normal for the second week at Wimbledon to be feeling niggling things on your muscles, because it's tough -- the grass, getting down low, coming into the net. It's more on the muscles than the joints on the grass,\" 2003 Wimbledon finalist Mark Philippoussis said. \"So I'm sure lots of people will be feeling it now with little things here and there.\"\n\nDjokovic can be counted among those who think part of the problem is that all of the rain during the tournament -- so much that the mixed doubles final was shifted from Thursday to Sunday, and play began a half-hour earlier than usual on most courts Wednesday -- has made the grass more slick and the footing less sure.\n\nSo has shutting the retractable roofs at Centre Court and No. 1 Court, the only two arenas at the All England Club with that luxury during wet weather.\n\n\"Once you close the roof, you know that the grass is going to be more slippery,\" Djokovic said. \"So there's more chances that players will fall. Unfortunately, some of the falls have caused some of the players to withdraw.\n\n\"It's part of this surface. You can't really change that. I mean, it's grass. It's a live surface, and it reacts to different conditions.\"\n\nAP photo by Kirsty Wigglesworth / Madison Keys leans over during her fourth-round match against Jasmine Paolini on Sunday at Wimbledon. Tiafoe played at Wimbledon with a black sleeve over his knee and made it to the third round before losing to reigning champion Carlos Alcaraz.\n\nDjokovic tore the meniscus in his right knee during a match at the French Open in early June, had surgery and returned to competition less than a month later. Ironically, he thinks it's possible that might have helped him stay upright in London.\n\nThat's because, years ago, Djokovic was one of the first players to regularly slide on grass the way they do on clay. He has cut down on those movements this time at Wimbledon, being extra careful to avoid risking falls.\n\n\"It's probably part of my, I guess, different kind of movement on the court that I've been really experimenting with because of the cautiousness -- because of the knee and everything that was happening prior to the tournament,\" Djokovic explained. \"The first couple rounds, I was still not maybe willing to go (for difficult) balls and slide and make splits.\"\n\nOther theories: More and more baseline play on grass, and less serve-and-volley play, creates longer points and extra running, which translate into a greater likelihood of slips; less comfort on grass because players tend to grow up practicing and competing on clay or hard courts; and a brief grass portion of the schedule that doesn't allow for accumulating a lot of experience on the turf.\n\nThen there's the general wear and tear of the season, which is more than halfway over at this point on the calendar.\n\n\"Listen, tennis is a very physical sport at the moment. Moments earlier, on Court 1, Bulgaria's Grigor Dimitrov had to retire due to injury after a slip in the first set against Daniil Medvedev, 28, left him limping.\n\nRelated: Emma Raducanu seeking her definitive Wimbledon moment\n\nThe roofs of Centre Court and Court 1 have been closed for much of the tournament as there were only two dry days in the first week. Eight players slipped while the roofs were covered on the show courts.\n\n\"Once you close the roof, it feels like we've been playing an indoor grass court tournament here more than [an] outdoor, to be honest. I played every single match basically indoor,\" Djokovic said after defeating Denmark's Holger Rune, 21, on Monday evening.\n\n\"But, once you close the roof, you know the grass is going to be more slippery. So there's more chances that players will fall. Unfortunately, some of the falls have caused some of the players to withdraw, like Dimitrov [on Sunday]. We saw [Alexander] Zverev as well in his previous match slightly injured his knee. That was troubling him even [on Monday].\n\n\"It's part of this surface. You can't really change that. I mean, it's grass. It's a live surface, and it reacts to different conditions, the humidity.\"\n\nSpeaking earlier on Monday, Sally Bolton, the chief executive of the All England Club (AELTC), denied the slips were due to the roofs being closed.\n\n\"The two show courts with the roofs, the courts are very dry, the roofs have not been open significantly - there isn't any dampness in there,\" she told reporters. \"In both show courts, we have a complex mechanism for keeping the air in the right condition for the court to be in the right condition.\n\n\"Those systems have been working very hard but they've been working fine so we have no concerns about the courts.\"\n\nRelated: Wimbledon diary: AI mishaps, clippings and reaction-speed testers\n\nShe said that the grass \"has got more moisture in it\" in the first few days of the championships, but that the courts tend to dry out as they wear.\n\n\"But that's the nature of grass courts themselves rather than any particular conditions as a result of the wet weather we've had,\" Bolton added.\n\nWimbledon has previously said 600 air distributors pump dry air into the courts to prevent condensation forming on the surface.\n\nGreg Rusedski, the former world No 4, defended the surface. \"It's called grass, you're supposed to slip,\" he told reporters as he prepared to take part in the mixed-invitation doubles.\n\n\"Everybody's like, 'Oh, there's a bad bounce.' God almighty - go and play in the 1990s and the last century. You're expecting bad bounces, it's not supposed to be perfect. Grass courts have never been better. Also, you've got to look at the way the grass works ... You're going to slip, that's part of the business and you've just got to deal with it.\"\n\nRelated: Rain, set and match: a day at Wimbledon 2024 - in pictures\n\nThe conditions also made headlines in 2021 after Serena Williams slipped and was forced to retire and Djokovic later complained that he could not remember falling over so many times on a court. {"uri": "8219125976", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "17:15:21", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T17:15:21Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T17:12:46Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.886274516582489, "url": "https://www.eagletribune.com/region/injuries-are-adding-up-at-wimbledon-and-determining-the-outcomes-of-matches/article_4994db88-ac08-56ca-8d27-c6c27808a98c.html", "title": "Injuries are adding up at Wimbledon and determining the outcomes of matches", "body": "Taylor Fritz of the United States falls as he attempts to make a backhand return to Alexander Zverev of Germany during their fourth round match at the Wimbledon tennis championships in London, Monday, July 8, 2024.\n\nLONDON (AP) -- There's no single explanation, of course, for all of the injuries to players in the latter stages at Wimbledon this year. This much is certain: The timing could hardly be worse.\n\nThe man Novak Djokovic was supposed to face on Wednesday, Alex de Minaur, withdrew hours before their scheduled quarterfinal because he jarred his hip at the end of a victory two days earlier.\n\n\"I'm devastated,\" de Minaur said. \"The problem with me going out and playing is that one stretch, one slide, one anything, can make this injury (recovery) go from three to six weeks to four months. It's too much to risk.\"\n\nTaylor Fritz's fourth-round opponent, Alexander Zverev, slipped on an unworn patch of green grass in his previous match. That caused a bone bruise -- and maybe worse -- that Zverev complained left him on \"one leg\" in his loss to the American at what the two-time major finalist characterized as a wide open opportunity to grab a first Grand Slam title.\n\nDanielle Collins' last Wimbledon appearance before retirement ended with tape wrapped around her hamstring, the work of a trainer during the American's fourth-round loss to 2021 French Open champion Barbora Krejcikova. Collins chalked it up to failing to \"think about every little step that you take.\"\n\n\"There's been a ton of injuries on the grass. Left and right, it seems like people are going down. I am, I guess, frustrated that I feel like I was focusing on my tactics and kind of what I needed to do to play at a high level. Usually, I feel like, on other surfaces you're not having to think so critically about your movement,\" Collins said. \"The one second I take my mind off of it, not think about every little thing I'm doing with my footwork, it ends up happening.\"\n\nThe falls keep happening. The injuries are adding up.\n\n\"It's unfortunate, obviously,\" de Minaur said. \"You never want to see this.\"\n\nHe called his mishap \"more of a freak injury,\" related to the \"excessive amount of force\" used to slide on grass.\n\nMadison Keys, the 2017 U.S. Open runner-up, was in tears when she stopped because of a hurt leg at 5-all in the third set of a Week 2 match against Jasmine Paolini, who reached Thursday's semifinals.\n\nEmma Raducanu, who won the U.S. Open three years ago, withdrew from mixed doubles -- which was supposed to be Andy Murray's last event at Wimbledon -- because of a sore wrist, then needed a medical timeout later that day after falling in the third set of a singles loss.\n\nNo. 17 Anna Kalinskaya cited a bad wrist when she quit in her fourth-round match against 2022 champion Elena Rybakina. No. 10 Grigor Dimitrov retired from his fourth-round match against Daniil Medvedev with a leg problem.\n\n\"It's normal for the second week at Wimbledon to be feeling niggling things on your muscles, because it's tough -- the grass, getting down low, coming into the net. It's more on the muscles than the joints on the grass,\" 2003 Wimbledon finalist Mark Philippoussis said. \"So I'm sure lots of people will be feeling it now with little things here and there.\"\n\nCount Djokovic among those who think part of the issue is that all of the rain during the tournament -- so much that the mixed doubles final was shifted from Thursday to Sunday, and play began a half-hour earlier than usual on most courts Wednesday -- has made the grass more slick and the footing less sure.\n\nSo has shutting the retractable roofs at Centre Court and No. 1 Court, the only two arenas with that luxury during wet weather.\n\n\"Once you close the roof, you know that the grass is going to be more slippery. So there's more chances that players will fall. Unfortunately, some of the falls have caused some of the players to withdraw,\" Djokovic said.\n\n\"It's part of this surface. You can't really change that,\" added the seven-time champion at the All England Club. \"I mean, it's grass. It's a live surface, and it reacts to different conditions.\"\n\nThe pattern began at grass tourneys that preceded Wimbledon.\n\nMarketa Vondrousova retired from a match in Berlin after hurting her right leg there. When she showed up at Wimbledon, she became the first defending champion in 30 years to lose in the first round and acknowledged: \"I was a bit scared because of my leg.\"\n\nThe woman who beat her last week, Jessica Bouzas Maneiro, stopped at Wimbledon because of a back issue in the third round against Krejcikova.\n\nFrances Tiafoe pulled out of the Queen's Club tournament before Wimbledon after spraining a ligament in his right knee when he took a tumble. Tiafoe played at the All England Club with a black sleeve over his knee and made it to the third round before losing to defending champion Carlos Alcaraz.\n\nDjokovic tore the meniscus in his right knee during a match at the French Open, had surgery and returned to competition less than a month later. Ironically, he thinks it's possible that might have helped him stay upright this fortnight.\n\nThat's because, years ago, Djokovic was one of the first players to regularly slide on grass the way they do on clay. He has cut down on those movements this time at Wimbledon, being extra careful to avoid risking falls.\n\n\"It's probably part of my, I guess, different kind of movement on the court that I've been really experimenting with because of the cautiousness -- because of the knee and everything that was happening prior to the tournament,\" Djokovic explained. \"The first couple rounds, I was still not maybe willing to go (for difficult) balls and slide and make splits.\"\n\nOther theories include: More and more baseline play on grass, and less serving-and-volleying, creates longer points and extra running, which translate into a greater likelihood of slips; less comfort on grass because players tend to grow up practicing and competing on clay or hard courts; and a brief grass portion of the schedule that doesn't allow for accumulating a lot of experience on the turf.\n\nThen there's the general wear-and-tear of a season.\n\n\"Listen, tennis is a very physical sport at the moment. For sure, the rallies are longer. Matches. Scheduling. Finishing late,\" 2006 Australian Open finalist Marcos Baghdatis said. \"It's very demanding on the body. ... A lot of things are changing that (contribute) to players getting injured.\"\n\nAP tennis: https://apnews.com/hub/tennis", "source": {"uri": "eagletribune.com", "dataType": "news", "title": He has cut down on those movements this time at Wimbledon, being extra careful to avoid risking falls.\n\n\"It's probably part of my, I guess, different kind of movement on the court that I've been really experimenting with because of the cautiousness -- because of the knee and everything that was happening prior to the tournament,\" Djokovic explained. \"The first couple rounds, I was still not maybe willing to go (for difficult) balls and slide and make splits.\"\n\nOther theories include: More and more baseline play on grass, and less serving-and-volleying, creates longer points and extra running, which translate into a greater likelihood of slips; less comfort on grass because players tend to grow up practicing and competing on clay or hard courts; and a brief grass portion of the schedule that doesn't allow for accumulating a lot of experience on the turf.\n\nThen there's the general wear-and-tear of a season.\n\n\"Listen, tennis is a very physical sport at the moment. For sure, the rallies are longer. Matches. Scheduling. Finishing late,\" 2006 Australian Open finalist Marcos Baghdatis said. \"It's very demanding on the body. ... A lot of things are changing that (contribute) to players getting injured.\"\n\nLove\n\n0\n\nFunny\n\n0\n\nWow\n\n0\n\nSad\n\n0\n\nAngry\n\n0\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to This much is certain: The timing could hardly be worse.\n\nThe man Novak Djokovic was supposed to face on Wednesday, Alex de Minaur, withdrew hours before their scheduled quarterfinal because he jarred his hip at the end of a victory two days earlier.\n\n\"I'm devastated,\" de Minaur said. \"The problem with me going out and playing is that one stretch, one slide, one anything, can make this injury (recovery) go from three to six weeks to four months. It's too much to risk.\"\n\nTaylor Fritz's fourth-round opponent, Alexander Zverev, slipped on an unworn patch of green grass in his previous match. That caused a bone bruise -- and maybe worse -- that Zverev complained left him on \"one leg\" in his loss to the American at what the twotime major finalist characterized as a wide open opportunity to grab a first Grand Slam title.\n\nPeople are also reading... TVA announces changes at South Holston, Boone properties LITTLE LEAGUE: Good day for local teams in state softball tournament McNew living her dream as a large truck mechanic 'Vampire' series gives Sam Reid plenty to bite into Simmons to expand manufacturing into Russell County AUTO RACING: Rural Retreat native Will Hostetler's a hometown hero at Wythe Raceway COLLEGE BASEBALL: Chase Hungate (Abingdon) headed to Mississippi State, relishes chance to compete in SEC PREP FOOTBALL: Shane Wicks gets first head-coaching opportunity at Honaker LITTLE LEAGUE: State tournaments set across region and state PREP FOOTBALL: The VHSL master schedule was released on Thursday. Here are some games with intriguing storylines COLLEGE FOOTBALL: You can find Brad Robbins (Powell Valley) coaching at UConn now LITTLE LEAGUE: Monday's State Tournament Results PREP FOOTBALL: Chris Long says Heath Miller is right man for job at St. Anne's-Belfield Scientists say they've confirmed a slowdown in Earth's inner core rotation. Now what? Marketplace dining opening at Hard Rock Bristol\n\nDanielle Collins' last Wimbledon appearance before retirement ended with tape wrapped around her hamstring, the work of a trainer during the American's fourth-round loss to 2021 French Open champion Barbora Krejcikova. Collins chalked it up to failing to \"think about every little step that you take.\"\n\n\"There's been a ton of injuries on the grass. Left and right, it seems like people are going down. I am, I guess, frustrated that I feel like I was focusing on my tactics and kind of what I needed to do to play at a high level. Usually, I feel like, on other surfaces you're not having to think so critically about your movement,\" Collins said. \"The one second I take my mind off of it, not think about every little thing I'm doing with my footwork, it ends up happening.\"\n\nThe falls keep happening. The injuries are adding up.\n\n\"It's unfortunate, obviously,\" de Minaur said. \"You never want to see this.\"\n\nHe called his mishap \"more of a freak injury,\" related to the \"excessive amount of force\" used to slide on grass.\n\nMadison Keys, the 2017 U.S. Open runner-up, was in tears when she stopped because of a hurt leg at 5-all in the third set of a Week 2 match against Jasmine Paolini, who reached Thursday's semifinals.\n\nEmma Raducanu, who won the U.S. Open three years ago, withdrew from mixed doubles -- which was supposed to be Andy Murray's last event at Wimbledon -- because of a sore wrist, then needed a medical timeout later that day after falling in the third set of a singles loss.\n\nNo. 17 Anna Kalinskaya cited a bad wrist when she quit in her fourth-round match against 2022 champion Elena Rybakina. No. 10 Grigor Dimitrov retired from his fourth-round match against Daniil Medvedev with a leg problem.\n\n\"It's normal for the second week at Wimbledon to be feeling niggling things on your muscles, because it's tough -- the grass, getting down low, coming into the net. It's more on the muscles than the joints on the grass,\" 2003 Wimbledon finalist Mark Philippoussis said. \"So I'm sure lots of people will be feeling it now with little things here and there.\"\n\nCount Djokovic among those who think part of the issue is that all of the rain during the tournament -- so much that the mixed doubles final was shifted from Thursday to Sunday, and play began a half-hour earlier than usual on most courts Wednesday -- has made the grass more slick and the footing less sure.\n\nSo has shutting the retractable roofs at Centre Court and No. 1 Court, the only two arenas with that luxury during wet weather.\n\n\"Once you close the roof, you know that the grass is going to be more slippery. So there's more chances that players will fall. Unfortunately, some of the falls have caused some of the players to withdraw,\" Djokovic said.\n\n\"It's part of this surface. You can't really change that,\" added the seven-time champion at the All England Club. \"I mean, it's grass. It's a live surface, and it reacts to different conditions.\"\n\nThe pattern began at grass tourneys that preceded Wimbledon.\n\nMarketa Vondrousova retired from a match in Berlin after hurting her right leg there. When she showed up at Wimbledon, she became the first defending champion in 30 years to lose in the first round and acknowledged: \"I was a bit scared because of my leg.\"\n\nThe woman who beat her last week, Jessica Bouzas Maneiro, stopped at Wimbledon because of a back issue in the third round against Krejcikova.\n\nFrances Tiafoe pulled out of the Queen's Club tournament before Wimbledon after spraining a ligament in his right knee when he took a tumble. Tiafoe played at the All England Club with a black sleeve over his knee and made it to the third round before losing to defending champion Carlos Alcaraz.\n\nDjokovic tore the meniscus in his right knee during a match at the French Open, had surgery and returned to competition less than a month later. Ironically, he thinks it's possible that might have helped him stay upright this fortnight.\n\nThat's because, years ago, Djokovic was one of the first players to regularly slide on grass the way they do on clay. He has cut down on those movements this time at Wimbledon, being extra careful to avoid risking falls.\n\n\"It's probably part of my, I guess, different kind of movement on the court that I've been really experimenting with because of the cautiousness -- because of the knee and everything that was happening prior to the tournament,\" Djokovic explained. \"The first couple rounds, I was still not maybe willing to go (for difficult) balls and slide and make splits.\"\n\nOther theories include: More and more baseline play on grass, and less serving-and-volleying, creates longer points and extra running, which translate into a greater likelihood of slips; less comfort on grass because players tend to grow up practicing and competing on clay or hard courts; and a brief grass portion of the schedule that doesn't allow for accumulating a lot of experience on the turf.\n\nThen there's the general wear-and-tear of a season.\n\n\"Listen, tennis is a very physical sport at the moment. For sure, the rallies are longer. Matches. Scheduling. Finishing late,\" 2006 Australian Open finalist Marcos Baghdatis said. \"It's very demanding on the body. ... A lot of things are changing that (contribute) to players getting injured.\"\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your inbox!\n\nSign up! * I understand and agree that registration {"uri": "8217246220", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-09", "time": "15:39:45", "dateTime": "2024-07-09T15:39:45Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-09T15:39:17Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8784313797950745, "url": "https://www.theguardian.com/sport/article/2024/jul/09/novak-djokovic-does-the-splits-as-latest-victim-of-rain-wimbledons-slippery-courts", "title": "Novak Djokovic does the splits as latest victim of Wimbledon's slippery courts", "body": "Former world No 1 blames the roofs being closed, saying grass becomes 'more slippery' and causes injuries\n\nWimbledon's slippery grass has been a long recurring theme, with Serena Williams and Andy Murray famously taking tumbles.\n\nNovak Djokovic, 37, has become the latest player to fall foul of the surface after he slipped and ended up doing the splits during his fourth-round match on Monday.\n\nThe former world No 1 blamed the roofs of the show courts being closed, saying the grass becomes \"more slippery\" and causes injuries.\n\nMore than a dozen players have slipped on the courts so far this year as nearly a month's worth of rain fell on the tournament in its first week.\n\nThe former US Open champion Emma Raducanu slipped and had to take a medical timeout in the third set of her fourth-round Centre Court clash against New Zealand's Lulu Sun, 23, on Sunday. Moments earlier, on Court 1, Bulgaria's Grigor Dimitrov had to retire due to injury after a slip in the first set against Daniil Medvedev, 28, left him limping.\n\nThe roofs of Centre Court and Court 1 have been closed for much of the tournament as there were only two dry days in the first week. Eight players slipped while the roofs were covered on the show courts.\n\n\"Once you close the roof, it feels like we've been playing an indoor grass court tournament here more than [an] outdoor, to be honest. I played every single match basically indoors,\" Djokovic said after defeating Denmark's Holger Rune, 21, on Monday evening.\n\n\"But, once you close the roof, you know the grass is going to be more slippery. So there's more chances that players will fall. Unfortunately, some of the falls have caused some of the players to withdraw, like Dimitrov [on Sunday]. We saw [Alexander] Zverev as well, in his previous match, slightly injured his knee. That was troubling him even [on Monday].\n\n\"It's part of this surface. You can't really change that. I mean, it's grass. It's a live surface, and it reacts to different conditions, the humidity.\"\n\nSpeaking earlier on Monday, Sally Bolton, the chief executive of the All England Club (AELTC), denied the slips were due to the roofs being closed.\n\n\"The two show courts with the roofs, the courts are very dry, the roofs have not been open significantly - there isn't any dampness in there,\" she told reporters. \"In both show courts, we have a complex mechanism for keeping the air in the right condition for the court to be in the right condition.\n\n\"Those systems have been working very hard but they've been working fine so we have no concerns about the courts.\"\n\nShe said that the grass \"has got more moisture in it\" in the first few days of the championships, but that the courts tend to dry out as they wear.\n\n\"But that's the nature of grass courts themselves rather than any particular conditions as a result of the wet weather we've had,\" Bolton added.\n\nWimbledon has previously said 600 air distributors pump dry air into the courts to prevent condensation forming on the surface.\n\nGreg Rusedski, the former world No 4, defended the surface. \"It's called grass, you're supposed to slip,\" he told reporters as he prepared to take part in the mixed-invitation doubles.\n\n\"Everybody's like, 'Oh, there's a bad bounce.' God almighty - go and play in the 1990s and the last century. You're expecting bad bounces, it's not supposed to be perfect. Grass courts have never been better. Also, you've got to look at the way the grass works ... You're going to slip, that's part of the business and you've just got to deal with it.\"\n\nThe conditions also made headlines in 2021 after Serena Williams slipped and was forced to retire and Djokovic later complained that he could not remember falling over so many times on a court. {"uri": "8219240234", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "18:48:56", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T18:48:56Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T18:48:19Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8745098114013672, "url": "https://wcfcourier.com/wimbledon-injuries-djokovic-de-minaur-collins-keys/article_8099ce2a-be0e-5432-a01e-359f5a1bc74a.html", "title": "Injuries are adding up at Wimbledon and determining the outcomes of matches", "body": "Danielle Collins plays a forehand return to Barbora Krejcikova of the Czech Republic during their fourth round match Monday at Wimbledon in London.\n\nHOWARD FENDRICH and KEN MAGUIRE Associated Press\n\nLONDON -- There's no single explanation, of course, for all of the injuries to players in the latter stages at Wimbledon this year. This much is certain: The timing could hardly be worse.\n\nThe man Novak Djokovic was supposed to face on Wednesday, Alex de Minaur, withdrew hours before their scheduled quarterfinal because he jarred his hip at the end of a victory two days earlier.\n\n\"I'm devastated,\" de Minaur said. \"The problem with me going out and playing is that one stretch, one slide, one anything, can make this injury (recovery) go from three to six weeks to four months. It's too much to risk.\"\n\nTaylor Fritz's fourth-round opponent, Alexander Zverev, slipped on an unworn patch of green grass in his previous match. That caused a bone bruise -- and maybe worse -- that Zverev complained left him on \"one leg\" in his loss to the American at what the two-time major finalist characterized as a wide open opportunity to grab a first Grand Slam title.\n\nPeople are also reading... Man shot outside bar in downtown Waterloo Ag expert: Deere prepares for future losses due to downturned ag economy Woman dead in single-vehicle accident John Deere closes on land for 1.2 million-square-foot Indiana warehouse Woman identified in fatal crash Waterloo man arrested after alleged assault at Sunnyside County Club Restaurant inspection update: Grimy blenders, mouse droppings and bad sushi Woman arrested for assaulting multiple people, punching officer, ramming property in Waterloo John Eveland: Waterloo businessman makes lasting impact in Cedar Valley community Firecracker Fourth of July celebrations planned throughout Cedar Valley Walnut Ridge Church to move to Tower Park Drive, future uncertain for private school space Bond remains same in pre-teen sex sting in Evansdale Student sentenced to probation in University of Northern Iowa Dance Marathon theft College Football: Cedar Falls OL, second Tiger commits to Northern Iowa football program UPDATE WITH NAME: One dead in tanker crash south of Hudson\n\nDanielle Collins' last Wimbledon appearance before retirement ended with tape wrapped around her hamstring, the work of a trainer during the American's fourth-round loss to 2021 French Open champion Barbora Krejcikova. Collins chalked it up to failing to \"think about every little step that you take.\"\n\n\"There's been a ton of injuries on the grass. Left and right, it seems like people are going down. I am, I guess, frustrated that I feel like I was focusing on my tactics and kind of what I needed to do to play at a high level. Usually, I feel like, on other surfaces you're not having to think so critically about your movement,\" Collins said. \"The one second I take my mind off of it, not think about every little thing I'm doing with my footwork, it ends up happening.\"\n\nThe falls keep happening. The injuries are adding up.\n\n\"It's unfortunate, obviously,\" de Minaur said. \"You never want to see this.\"\n\nHe called his mishap \"more of a freak injury,\" related to the \"excessive amount of force\" used to slide on grass.\n\nMadison Keys, the 2017 U.S. Open runner-up, was in tears when she stopped because of a hurt leg at 5-all in the third set of a Week 2 match against Jasmine Paolini, who reached Thursday's semifinals.\n\nEmma Raducanu, who won the U.S. Open three years ago, withdrew from mixed doubles -- which was supposed to be Andy Murray's last event at Wimbledon -- because of a sore wrist, then needed a medical timeout later that day after falling in the third set of a singles loss.\n\nNo. 17 Anna Kalinskaya cited a bad wrist when she quit in her fourth-round match against 2022 champion Elena Rybakina. No. 10 Grigor Dimitrov retired from his fourth-round match against Daniil Medvedev with a leg problem.\n\n\"It's normal for the second week at Wimbledon to be feeling niggling things on your muscles, because it's tough -- the grass, getting down low, coming into the net. It's more on the muscles than the joints on the grass,\" 2003 Wimbledon finalist Mark Philippoussis said. \"So I'm sure lots of people will be feeling it now with little things here and there.\"\n\nCount Djokovic among those who think part of the issue is that all of the rain during the tournament -- so much that the mixed doubles final was shifted from Thursday to Sunday, and play began a half-hour earlier than usual on most courts Wednesday -- has made the grass more slick and the footing less sure.\n\nSo has shutting the retractable roofs at Centre Court and No. 1 Court, the only two arenas with that luxury during wet weather.\n\n\"Once you close the roof, you know that the grass is going to be more slippery. So there's more chances that players will fall. Unfortunately, some of the falls have caused some of the players to withdraw,\" Djokovic said.\n\n\"It's part of this surface. You can't really change that,\" added the seven-time champion at the All England Club. \"I mean, it's grass. It's a live surface, and it reacts to different conditions.\"\n\nThe pattern began at grass tourneys that preceded Wimbledon.\n\nMarketa Vondrousova retired from a match in Berlin after hurting her right leg there. When she showed up at Wimbledon, she became the first defending champion in 30 years to lose in the first round and acknowledged: \"I was a bit scared because of my leg.\"\n\nThe woman who beat her last week, Jessica Bouzas Maneiro, stopped at Wimbledon because of a back issue in the third round against Krejcikova.\n\nFrances Tiafoe pulled out of the Queen's Club tournament before Wimbledon after spraining a ligament in his right knee when he took a tumble. Tiafoe played at the All England Club with a black sleeve over his knee and made it to the third round before losing to defending champion Carlos Alcaraz.\n\nDjokovic tore the meniscus in his right knee during a match at the French Open, had surgery and returned to competition less than a month later. Ironically, he thinks it's possible that might have helped him stay upright this fortnight.\n\nThat's because, years ago, Djokovic was one of the first players to regularly slide on grass the way they do on clay. He has cut down on those movements this time at Wimbledon, being extra careful to avoid risking falls.\n\n\"It's probably part of my, I guess, different kind of movement on the court that I've been really experimenting with because of the cautiousness -- because of the knee and everything that was happening prior to the tournament,\" Djokovic explained. \"The first couple rounds, I was still not maybe willing to go (for difficult) balls and slide and make splits.\"\n\nOther theories include: More and more baseline play on grass, and less serving-and-volleying, creates longer points and extra running, which translate into a greater likelihood of slips; less comfort on grass because players tend to grow up practicing and competing on clay or hard courts; and a brief grass portion of the schedule that doesn't allow for accumulating a lot of experience on the turf.\n\nThen there's the general wear-and-tear of a season.\n\n\"Listen, tennis is a very physical sport at the moment. For sure, the rallies are longer. Matches. Scheduling. Finishing late,\" 2006 Australian Open finalist Marcos Baghdatis said. \"It's very demanding on the body. ... A lot of things are changing that (contribute) to players getting injured.\"\n\n0 Comments Love 0 Funny 0 Wow 0 Sad 0 Angry 0\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your inbox!\n\nSign This much is certain: The timing could hardly be worse.\n\nThe man Novak Djokovic was supposed to face on Wednesday, Alex de Minaur, withdrew hours before their scheduled quarterfinal because he jarred his hip at the end of a victory two days earlier.\n\n\"I'm devastated,\" de Minaur said. \"The problem with me going out and playing is that one stretch, one slide, one anything, can make this injury (recovery) go from three to six weeks to four months. It's too much to risk.\"\n\nTaylor Fritz's fourth-round opponent, Alexander Zverev, slipped on an unworn patch of green grass in his previous match. That caused a bone bruise -- and maybe worse -- that Zverev complained left him on \"one leg\" in his loss to the American at what the two-time major finalist characterized as a wide open opportunity to grab a first Grand Slam title.\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\nSteve Cahalan: Batter and Brew hits the streets in La Crosse Steve Cahalan: Downtown Holiday Inn converting to boutique hotel in La Crosse Steve Cahalan: Crumbl Cookies to begin construction in late summer in Onalaska Local talent shines in first-ever Riverfest Idol karaoke tournament One injured in three vehicle crash near Arcadia Galesville man charged with eluding police and stealing vehicle in Onalaska Skyrockers firework show postponed; more closures due to rain and flooding UPDATE: $500K bond set for man charged with assault in Winona that led to shelter-in-place order Two spills of chemical firefighting foam occurred at La Crosse Regional Airport earlier this year, reports say Criminal complaint implicates two La Crosse residents for human trafficking Human trafficking in La Crosse: Police department raises awareness after recent arrests Tellurian awarded $35,000 from Mayo Health Viroqua's fireworks show postponed due to inclement weather Spring boys all-conference teams: Complete Coulee Region listing Amtrak reports strong ticket sales for new line running through Wisconsin\n\nDanielle Collins' last Wimbledon appearance before retirement ended with tape wrapped around her hamstring, the work of a trainer during the American's fourth-round loss to 2021 French Open champion Barbora Krejcikova. Collins chalked it up to failing to \"think about every little step that you take.\"\n\n\"There's been a ton of injuries on the grass. Left and right, it seems like people are going down. I am, I guess, frustrated that I feel like I was focusing on my tactics and kind of what I needed to do to play at a high level. Usually, I feel like, on other surfaces you're not having to think so critically about your movement,\" Collins said. \"The one second I take my mind off of it, not think about every little thing I'm doing with my footwork, it ends up happening.\"\n\nThe falls keep happening. The injuries are adding up.\n\n\"It's unfortunate, obviously,\" de Minaur said. \"You never want to see this.\"\n\nHe called his mishap \"more of a freak injury,\" related to the \"excessive amount of force\" used to slide on grass.\n\nMadison Keys, the 2017 U.S. Open runner-up, was in tears when she stopped because of a hurt leg at 5-all in the third set of a Week 2 match against Jasmine Paolini, who reached Thursday's semifinals.\n\nEmma Raducanu, who won the U.S. Open three years ago, withdrew from mixed doubles -- which was supposed to be Andy Murray's last event at Wimbledon -- because of a sore wrist, then needed a medical timeout later that day after falling in the third set of a singles loss.\n\nNo. 17 Anna Kalinskaya cited a bad wrist when she quit in her fourth-round match against 2022 champion Elena Rybakina. No. 10 Grigor Dimitrov retired from his fourth-round match against Daniil Medvedev with a leg problem.\n\n\"It's normal for the second week at Wimbledon to be feeling niggling things on your muscles, because it's tough -- the grass, getting down low, coming into the net. It's more on the muscles than the joints on the grass,\" 2003 Wimbledon finalist Mark Philippoussis said. \"So I'm sure lots of people will be feeling it now with little things here and there.\"\n\nCount Djokovic among those who think part of the issue is that all of the rain during the tournament -- so much that the mixed doubles final was shifted from Thursday to Sunday, and play began a half-hour earlier than usual on most courts Wednesday -- has made the grass more slick and the footing less sure.\n\nSo has shutting the retractable roofs at Centre Court and No. 1 Court, the only two arenas with that luxury during wet weather.\n\n\"Once you close the roof, you know that the grass is going to be more slippery. So there's more chances that players will fall. Unfortunately, some of the falls have caused some of the players to withdraw,\" Djokovic said.\n\n\"It's part of this surface. You can't really change that,\" added the seven-time champion at the All England Club. \"I mean, it's grass. It's a live surface, and it reacts to different conditions.\"\n\nThe pattern began at grass tourneys that preceded Wimbledon.\n\nMarketa Vondrousova retired from a match in Berlin after hurting her right leg there. When she showed up at Wimbledon, she became the first defending champion in 30 years to lose in the first round and acknowledged: \"I was a bit scared because of my leg.\"\n\nThe woman who beat her last week, Jessica Bouzas Maneiro, stopped at Wimbledon because of a back issue in the third round against Krejcikova.\n\nFrances Tiafoe pulled out of the Queen's Club tournament before Wimbledon after spraining a ligament in his right knee when he took a tumble. Tiafoe played at the All England Club with a black sleeve over his knee and made it to the third round before losing to defending champion Carlos Alcaraz.\n\nDjokovic tore the meniscus in his right knee during a match at the French Open, had surgery and returned to competition less than a month later. Ironically, he thinks it's possible that might have helped him stay upright this fortnight.\n\nThat's because, years ago, Djokovic was one of the first players to regularly slide on grass the way they do on clay. He has cut down on those movements this time at Wimbledon, being extra careful to avoid risking falls.\n\n\"It's probably part of my, I guess, different kind of movement on the court that I've been really experimenting with because of the cautiousness -- because of the knee and everything that was happening prior to the tournament,\" Djokovic explained. \"The first couple rounds, I was still not maybe willing to go (for difficult) balls and slide and make splits.\"\n\nOther theories include: More and more baseline play on grass, and less serving-and-volleying, creates longer points and extra running, which translate into a greater likelihood of slips; less comfort on grass because players tend to grow up practicing and competing on clay or hard courts; and a brief grass portion of the schedule that doesn't allow for accumulating a lot of experience on the turf.\n\nThen there's the general wear-and-tear of a season.\n\n\"Listen, tennis is a very physical sport at the moment. For sure, the rallies are longer. Matches. Scheduling. Finishing late,\" 2006 Australian Open finalist Marcos Baghdatis said. \"It's very demanding on the body. ... A lot of things are changing that (contribute) to players getting injured.\"\n\nLove\n\n0\n\nFunny\n\n0\n\nWow\n\n0\n\nSad\n\n0\n\nAngry\n\n0\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to This much is certain: The timing could hardly be worse.\n\nThe man Novak Djokovic was supposed to face on Wednesday, Alex de Minaur, withdrew hours before their scheduled quarterfinal because he jarred his hip at the end of a victory two days earlier.\n\n\"I'm devastated,\" de Minaur said. \"The problem with me going out and playing is that one stretch, one slide, one anything, can make this injury (recovery) go from three to six weeks to four months. It's more on the muscles than the joints on the grass,\" 2003 Wimbledon finalist Mark Philippoussis said. \"So I'm sure lots of people will be feeling it now with little things here and there.\"\n\nCount Djokovic among those who think part of the issue is that all of the rain during the tournament -- so much that the mixed doubles final was shifted from Thursday to Sunday, and play began a half-hour earlier than usual on most courts Wednesday -- has made the grass more slick and the footing less sure.\n\nSo has shutting the retractable roofs at Centre Court and No. 1 Court, the only two arenas with that luxury during wet weather.\n\n\"Once you close the roof, you know that the grass is going to be more slippery. When she showed up at Wimbledon, she became the first defending champion in 30 years to lose in the first round and acknowledged: \"I was a bit scared because of my leg.\"\n\nThe woman who beat her last week, Jessica Bouzas Maneiro, stopped at Wimbledon because of a back issue in the third round against Krejcikova.\n\nFrances Tiafoe pulled out of the Queen's Club tournament before Wimbledon after spraining a ligament in his right knee when he took a tumble. Tiafoe played at the All England Club with a black sleeve over his knee and made it to the third round before losing to defending champion Carlos Alcaraz.\n\nDjokovic tore the meniscus in his right knee during a match at the French Open, had surgery and returned to competition less than a month later. Ironically, he thinks it's possible that might have helped him stay upright this fortnight.\n\nThat's because, years ago, Djokovic was one of the first players to regularly slide on grass the way they do on clay. He has cut down on those movements this time at Wimbledon, being extra careful to avoid risking falls.\n\n\"It's probably part of my, I guess, different kind of movement on the court that I've been really experimenting with because of the cautiousness -- because of the knee and everything that was happening prior to the tournament,\" Djokovic explained. \"The first couple rounds, I was still not maybe willing to go (for difficult) balls and slide and make splits.\"\n\nOther theories include: More and more baseline play on grass, and less serving-and-volleying, creates longer points and extra running, which translate into a greater likelihood of slips; less comfort on grass because players tend to grow up practicing and competing on clay or hard courts; and a brief grass portion of the schedule that doesn't allow for accumulating a lot of experience on the turf.\n\nThen there's the general wear-and-tear of a season.\n\n\"Listen, tennis is a very physical sport at the moment. For sure, the rallies are longer. Matches. Scheduling. Finishing late,\" 2006 Australian Open finalist Marcos Baghdatis said. \"It's very demanding on the body. ... {"uri": "8219150727", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "17:32:47", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T17:32:47Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T17:32:04Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8627451062202454, "url": "https://pantagraph.com/wimbledon-injuries-djokovic-de-minaur-collins-keys/article_93aef2df-dcd6-5928-948b-53ee63cc5671.html", "title": "Injuries are adding up at Wimbledon and determining the outcomes of matches", "body": "Novak Djokovic of Serbia falls during Monday's fourth round match against Holger Rune of Denmark at Wimbledon in London.\n\nHOWARD FENDRICH and KEN MAGUIRE Associated Press\n\nLONDON -- There's no single explanation, of course, for all of the injuries to players in the latter stages at Wimbledon this year. This much is certain: The timing could hardly be worse.\n\nThe man Novak Djokovic was supposed to face on Wednesday, Alex de Minaur, withdrew hours before their scheduled quarterfinal because he jarred his hip at the end of a victory two days earlier.\n\n\"I'm devastated,\" de Minaur said. \"The problem with me going out and playing is that one stretch, one slide, one anything, can make this injury (recovery) go from three to six weeks to four months. It's too much to risk.\"\n\nTaylor Fritz's fourth-round opponent, Alexander Zverev, slipped on an unworn patch of green grass in his previous match. That caused a bone bruise -- and maybe worse -- that Zverev complained left him on \"one leg\" in his loss to the American at what the two-time major finalist characterized as a wide open opportunity to grab a first Grand Slam title.\n\nPeople are also reading... Man found dead behind the wheel of vehicle on Veterans Parkway, police say 8-year-old boy dies after Wednesday night crash on I-74 Body pulled from Illinois River identified as Bloomington teen Bloomington woman faces DUI in crash that killed her 8-year-old son Flick Fact: How many State Farm employees are college educated? Bloomington man jailed on weapons, drug charges after traffic stop LaSalle County man dies in illegal fireworks incident Pub II marking 50th anniversary with party in uptown Normal Flick Fact: The $800M ex-farm girl from Stanford and Olympia High? Man found unresponsive in Bloomington had suffered medical event, police said McLean County man detained on cocaine sales charges Behind the Pritzker administration's quest to signal Illinois is 'open for business' Funerals pending for July 4 Funerals pending for July 5 Opening statements begin in trial of man accused of killing teen at NYE party in Normal\n\nDanielle Collins' last Wimbledon appearance before retirement ended with tape wrapped around her hamstring, the work of a trainer during the American's fourth-round loss to 2021 French Open champion Barbora Krejcikova. Collins chalked it up to failing to \"think about every little step that you take.\"\n\n\"There's been a ton of injuries on the grass. Left and right, it seems like people are going down. I am, I guess, frustrated that I feel like I was focusing on my tactics and kind of what I needed to do to play at a high level. Usually, I feel like, on other surfaces you're not having to think so critically about your movement,\" Collins said. \"The one second I take my mind off of it, not think about every little thing I'm doing with my footwork, it ends up happening.\"\n\nThe falls keep happening. The injuries are adding up.\n\n\"It's unfortunate, obviously,\" de Minaur said. \"You never want to see this.\"\n\nHe called his mishap \"more of a freak injury,\" related to the \"excessive amount of force\" used to slide on grass.\n\nMadison Keys, the 2017 U.S. Open runner-up, was in tears when she stopped because of a hurt leg at 5-all in the third set of a Week 2 match against Jasmine Paolini, who reached Thursday's semifinals.\n\nEmma Raducanu, who won the U.S. Open three years ago, withdrew from mixed doubles -- which was supposed to be Andy Murray's last event at Wimbledon -- because of a sore wrist, then needed a medical timeout later that day after falling in the third set of a singles loss.\n\nNo. 17 Anna Kalinskaya cited a bad wrist when she quit in her fourth-round match against 2022 champion Elena Rybakina. No. 10 Grigor Dimitrov retired from his fourth-round match against Daniil Medvedev with a leg problem.\n\n\"It's normal for the second week at Wimbledon to be feeling niggling things on your muscles, because it's tough -- the grass, getting down low, coming into the net. It's more on the muscles than the joints on the grass,\" 2003 Wimbledon finalist Mark Philippoussis said. \"So I'm sure lots of people will be feeling it now with little things here and there.\"\n\nCount Djokovic among those who think part of the issue is that all of the rain during the tournament -- so much that the mixed doubles final was shifted from Thursday to Sunday, and play began a half-hour earlier than usual on most courts Wednesday -- has made the grass more slick and the footing less sure.\n\nSo has shutting the retractable roofs at Centre Court and No. 1 Court, the only two arenas with that luxury during wet weather.\n\n\"Once you close the roof, you know that the grass is going to be more slippery. So there's more chances that players will fall. Unfortunately, some of the falls have caused some of the players to withdraw,\" Djokovic said.\n\n\"It's part of this surface. You can't really change that,\" added the seven-time champion at the All England Club. \"I mean, it's grass. It's a live surface, and it reacts to different conditions.\"\n\nThe pattern began at grass tourneys that preceded Wimbledon.\n\nMarketa Vondrousova retired from a match in Berlin after hurting her right leg there. When she showed up at Wimbledon, she became the first defending champion in 30 years to lose in the first round and acknowledged: \"I was a bit scared because of my leg.\"\n\nThe woman who beat her last week, Jessica Bouzas Maneiro, stopped at Wimbledon because of a back issue in the third round against Krejcikova.\n\nFrances Tiafoe pulled out of the Queen's Club tournament before Wimbledon after spraining a ligament in his right knee when he took a tumble. Tiafoe played at the All England Club with a black sleeve over his knee and made it to the third round before losing to defending champion Carlos Alcaraz.\n\nDjokovic tore the meniscus in his right knee during a match at the French Open, had surgery and returned to competition less than a month later. Ironically, he thinks it's possible that might have helped him stay upright this fortnight.\n\nThat's because, years ago, Djokovic was one of the first players to regularly slide on grass the way they do on clay. He has cut down on those movements this time at Wimbledon, being extra careful to avoid risking falls.\n\n\"It's probably part of my, I guess, different kind of movement on the court that I've been really experimenting with because of the cautiousness -- because of the knee and everything that was happening prior to the tournament,\" Djokovic explained. \"The first couple rounds, I was still not maybe willing to go (for difficult) balls and slide and make splits.\"\n\nOther theories include: More and more baseline play on grass, and less serving-and-volleying, creates longer points and extra running, which translate into a greater likelihood of slips; less comfort on grass because players tend to grow up practicing and competing on clay or hard courts; and a brief grass portion of the schedule that doesn't allow for accumulating a lot of experience on the turf.\n\nThen there's the general wear-and-tear of a season.\n\n\"Listen, tennis is a very physical sport at the moment. For sure, the rallies are longer. Matches. Scheduling. Finishing late,\" 2006 Australian Open finalist Marcos Baghdatis said. \"It's very demanding on the body. ... A lot of things are changing that (contribute) to players getting injured.\"\n\nLove 0 Funny 0 Wow 0 Sad 0 Angry 0\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your inbox!\n\nSign up! * I Now what?\n\nDanielle Collins' last Wimbledon appearance before retirement ended with tape wrapped around her hamstring, the work of a trainer during the American's fourth-round loss to 2021 French Open champion Barbora Krejcikova. Collins chalked it up to failing to \"think about every little step that you take.\"\n\n\"There's been a ton of injuries on the grass. Left and right, it seems like people are going down. I am, I guess, frustrated that I feel like I was focusing on my tactics and kind of what I needed to do to play at a high level. Usually, I feel like, on other surfaces you're not having to think so critically about your movement,\" Collins said. \"The one second I take my mind off of it, not think about every little thing I'm doing with my footwork, it ends up happening.\"\n\nThe falls keep happening. The injuries are adding up.\n\n\"It's unfortunate, obviously,\" de Minaur said. \"You never want to see this.\"\n\nHe called his mishap \"more of a freak injury,\" related to the \"excessive amount of force\" used to slide on grass.\n\nMadison Keys, the 2017 U.S. Open runner-up, was in tears when she stopped because of a hurt leg at 5-all in the third set of a Week 2 match against Jasmine Paolini, who reached Thursday's semifinals.\n\nEmma Raducanu, who won the U.S. Open three years ago, withdrew from mixed doubles -- which was supposed to be Andy Murray's last event at Wimbledon -- because of a sore wrist, then needed a medical timeout later that day after falling in the third set of a singles loss.\n\nNo. 17 Anna Kalinskaya cited a bad wrist when she quit in her fourth-round match against 2022 champion Elena Rybakina. It's more on the muscles than the joints on the grass,\" 2003 Wimbledon finalist Mark Philippoussis said. \"So I'm sure lots of people will be feeling it now with little things here and there.\"\n\nCount Djokovic among those who think part of the issue is that all of the rain during the tournament -- so much that the mixed doubles final was shifted from Thursday to Sunday, and play began a half-hour earlier than usual on most courts Wednesday -- has made the grass more slick and the footing less sure.\n\nSo has shutting the retractable roofs at Centre Court and No. 1 Court, the only two arenas with that luxury during wet weather.\n\n\"Once you close the roof, you know that the grass is going to be more slippery. So there's more chances that players will fall. Unfortunately, some of the falls have caused some of the players to withdraw,\" Djokovic said.\n\n\"It's part of this surface. You can't really change that,\" added the seven-time champion at the All England Club. \"I mean, it's grass. It's a live surface, and it reacts to different conditions.\"\n\nThe pattern began at grass tourneys that preceded Wimbledon.\n\nMarketa Vondrousova retired from a match in Berlin after hurting her right leg there. When she showed up at Wimbledon, she became the first defending champion in 30 years to lose in the first round and acknowledged: \"I was a bit scared because of my leg.\"\n\nThe woman who beat her last week, Jessica Bouzas Maneiro, stopped at Wimbledon because of a back issue in the third round against Krejcikova.\n\nFrances Tiafoe pulled out of the Queen's Club tournament before Wimbledon after spraining a ligament in his right knee when he took a tumble. Tiafoe played at the All England Club with a black sleeve over his knee and made it to the third round before losing to defending champion Carlos Alcaraz.\n\nDjokovic tore the meniscus in his right knee during a match at the French Open, had surgery and returned to competition less than a month later. Ironically, he thinks it's possible that might have helped him stay upright this fortnight.\n\nThat's because, years ago, Djokovic was one of the first players to regularly slide on grass the way they do on clay. He has cut down on those movements this time at Wimbledon, being extra careful to avoid risking falls.\n\n\"It's probably part of my, I guess, different kind of movement on the court that I've been really experimenting with because of the cautiousness -- because of the knee and everything that was happening prior to the tournament,\" Djokovic explained. \"The first couple rounds, I was still not maybe willing to go (for difficult) balls and slide and make splits.\"\n\nOther theories include: More and more baseline play on grass, and less serving-and-volleying, creates longer points and extra running, which translate into a greater likelihood of slips; less comfort on grass because players tend to grow up practicing and competing on clay or hard courts; and a brief grass portion of the schedule that doesn't allow for accumulating a lot of experience on the turf.\n\nThen there's the general wear-and-tear of a season.\n\n\"Listen, tennis is a very physical sport at the moment. For sure, the rallies are longer. Matches. Scheduling. Finishing late,\" 2006 Australian Open finalist Marcos Baghdatis said. \"It's very demanding on the body. ... A lot of things are changing that (contribute) to players getting injured.\"\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your inbox!\n\nSign up! * I understand and agree that registration This much is certain: The timing could hardly be worse.\n\nThe man Novak Djokovic was supposed to face on Wednesday, Alex de Minaur, withdrew hours before their scheduled quarterfinal because he jarred his hip at the end of a victory two days earlier.\n\n\"I'm devastated,\" de Minaur said. \"The problem with me going out and playing is that one stretch, one slide, one anything, can make this injury (recovery) go from three to six weeks to four months. It's too much to risk.\"\n\nTaylor Fritz's fourth-round opponent, Alexander Zverev, slipped on an unworn patch of green grass in his previous match. That caused a bone bruise -- and maybe worse -- that Zverev complained left him on \"one leg\" in his loss to the American at what the two-time major finalist characterized as a wide open opportunity to grab a first Grand Slam title.\n\nPeople are also reading... Napa's first Fourth of July drone show takes to the sky amid mixed feelings A Napa teen was killed by gun violence. Her life inspired a scholarship. Document at center of Supervisor Ramos' restraining order found in public filings Supervisor Belia Ramos files court documents alleging harassment Judge considers vacating temporary restraining order obtained by Supervisor Belia Ramos Napa judge declines to remove temporary restraining order obtained by Supervisor Belia Ramos St. Helena Cyclery to reopen in wake of Trek's closure Fire hazards and federal subpoenas: Questions continue to swirl around Clover Flat Landfill Manslaughter case over cyclist couple killed in Napa moves forward Napa Farmers Market: There's always a place for you at the market Bottoms Up!: A converted horse trailer bar for hire in Napa Hank McGraw, onetime baseball phenom turned maverick and late-life Napan, dead at 81 Far Niente Wine Estates to welcome third venture in September St. Helena's V. Sattui Winery sued by former employee alleging wage abuse Update: Silverado Trail reopens in Napa County after two fires reported\n\nDanielle Collins' last Wimbledon appearance before retirement ended with tape wrapped around her hamstring, the work of a trainer during the American's fourth-round loss to 2021 French Open champion Barbora Krejcikova. Collins chalked it up to failing to \"think about every little step that you take.\"\n\n\"There's been a ton of injuries on the grass. Left and right, it seems like people are going down. I am, I guess, frustrated that I feel like I was focusing on my tactics and kind of what I needed to do to play at a high level. Usually, I feel like, on other surfaces you're not having to think so critically about your movement,\" Collins said. \"The one second I take my mind off of it, not think about every little thing I'm doing with my footwork, it ends up happening.\"\n\nThe falls keep happening. The injuries are adding up.\n\n\"It's unfortunate, obviously,\" de Minaur said. \"You never want to see this.\"\n\nHe called his mishap \"more of a freak injury,\" related to the \"excessive amount of force\" used to slide on grass.\n\nMadison Keys, the 2017 U.S. Open runner-up, was in tears when she stopped because of a hurt leg at 5-all in the third set of a Week 2 match against Jasmine Paolini, who reached Thursday's semifinals.\n\nEmma Raducanu, who won the U.S. Open three years ago withdrew from mixed doubles -- which was supposed to be Andy Murray's last event at Wimbledon -- because of a sore wrist, then needed a medical timeout later that day after falling in the third set of a singles loss.\n\nNo. 17 Anna Kalinskaya cited a bad wrist when she quit in her fourth-round match against 2022 champion Elena Rybakina. No. 10 Grigor Dimitrov retired from his fourth-round match against Daniil Medvedev with a leg problem.\n\n\"It's normal for the second week at Wimbledon to be feeling niggling things on your muscles, because it's tough -- the grass, getting down low, coming into the net. It's more on the muscles than the joints on the grass,\" 2003 Wimbledon finalist Mark Philippoussis said. \"So I'm sure lots of people will be feeling it now with little things here and there.\"\n\nCount Djokovic among those who think part of the issue is that all of the rain during the tournament -- so much that the mixed doubles final was shifted from Thursday to Sunday, and play began a half-hour earlier than usual on most courts Wednesday -- has made the grass more slick and the footing less sure.\n\nSo has shutting the retractable roofs at Centre Court and No. 1 Court, the only two arenas with that luxury during wet weather.\n\n\"Once you close the roof, you know that the grass is going to be more slippery. So there's more chances that players will fall. Unfortunately, some of the falls have caused some of the players to withdraw,\" Djokovic said.\n\n\"It's part of this surface. You can't really change that,\" added the seven-time champion at the All England Club. \"I mean, it's grass. It's a live surface, and it reacts to different conditions.\"\n\nThe pattern began at grass tourneys that preceded Wimbledon.\n\nMarketa Vondrousova retired from a match in Berlin after hurting her right leg there. When she showed up at Wimbledon, she became the first defending champion in 30 years to lose in the first round and acknowledged: \"I was a bit scared because of my leg.\"\n\nThe woman who beat her last week, Jessica Bouzas Maneiro, stopped at Wimbledon because of a back issue in the third round against Krejcikova.\n\nFrances Tiafoe pulled out of the Queen's Club tournament before Wimbledon after spraining a ligament in his right knee when he took a tumble. Tiafoe played at the All England Club with a black sleeve over his knee and made it to the third round before losing to defending champion Carlos Alcaraz.\n\nDjokovic tore the meniscus in his right knee during a match at the French Open, had surgery and returned to competition less than a month later. Ironically, he thinks it's possible that might have helped him stay upright this fortnight.\n\nThat's because, years ago, Djokovic was one of the first players to regularly slide on grass the way they do on clay. He has cut down on those movements this time at Wimbledon, being extra careful to avoid risking falls.\n\n\"It's probably part of my, I guess, different kind of movement on the court that I've been really experimenting with because of the cautiousness -- because of the knee and everything that was happening prior to the tournament,\" Djokovic explained.\n\n\"The first couple rounds, I was still not maybe willing to go (for difficult) balls and slide and make splits.\"\n\nOther theories include: More and more baseline play on grass, and less serving-and-volleying, creates longer points and extra running, which translate into a greater likelihood of slips; less comfort on grass because players tend to grow up practicing and competing on clay or hard courts; and a brief grass portion of the schedule that doesn't allow for accumulating a lot of experience on the turf.\n\nThen there's the general wear-and-tear of a season.\n\n\"Listen, tennis is a very physical sport at the moment. For sure, the rallies are longer. Matches. Scheduling. Finishing late,\" 2006 Australian Open finalist Marcos Baghdatis said. \"It's very demanding on the body. ... A lot of things are changing that (contribute) to players getting injured.\"\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your inbox!\n\nSign up! * I understand and agree that registration
