{"uri": "2024-07-417522220", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "13:52:16", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T13:52:16Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T09:27:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8470588326454163, "url": "https://www.usnews.com/news/sports/articles/2024-07-10/injures-are-adding-up-at-wimbledon-and-determining-the-outcomes-of-matches", "title": "Injures Are Adding up at Wimbledon and Determining the Outcomes of Matches", "body": "LONDON (AP) -- There's no single explanation, of course, for all of the injuries to players in the latter stages at Wimbledon this year. This much is certain: The timing could hardly be worse.\n\nThe man Novak Djokovic was supposed to face on Wednesday, Alex de Minaur, withdrew hours before their scheduled quarterfinal because he jarred his hip at the end of a victory two days earlier.\n\n\"I'm devastated,\" de Minaur said. \"The problem with me going out and playing is that one stretch, one slide, one anything, can make this injury (recovery) go from three to six weeks to four months. It's too much to risk.\"\n\nTaylor Fritz's fourth-round opponent, Alexander Zverev, slipped on an unworn patch of green grass in his previous match. That caused a bone bruise -- and maybe worse -- that Zverev complained left him on \"one leg\" in his loss to the American at what the two-time major finalist characterized as a wide open opportunity to grab a first Grand Slam title.\n\nDanielle Collins' last Wimbledon appearance before retirement ended with tape wrapped around her hamstring, the work of a trainer during the American's fourth-round loss to 2021 French Open champion Barbora Krejcikova. Collins chalked it up to failing to \"think about every little step that you take.\"\n\n\"There's been a ton of injuries on the grass. Left and right, it seems like people are going down. I am, I guess, frustrated that I feel like I was focusing on my tactics and kind of what I needed to do to play at a high level. Usually, I feel like, on other surfaces you're not having to think so critically about your movement,\" Collins said. \"The one second I take my mind off of it, not think about every little thing I'm doing with my footwork, it ends up happening.\"\n\nThe falls keep happening. The injuries are adding up.\n\n\"It's unfortunate, obviously,\" de Minaur said. \"You never want to see this.\"\n\nHe called his mishap \"more of a freak injury,\" related to the \"excessive amount of force\" used to slide on grass.\n\nMadison Keys, the 2017 U.S. Open runner-up, was in tears when she stopped because of a hurt leg at 5-all in the third set of a Week 2 match against Jasmine Paolini, who reached Thursday's semifinals.\n\nEmma Raducanu, who won the U.S. Open three years ago, withdrew from mixed doubles -- which was supposed to be Andy Murray's last event at Wimbledon -- because of a sore wrist, then needed a medical timeout later that day after falling in the third set of a singles loss.\n\nNo. 17 Anna Kalinskaya cited a bad wrist when she quit in her fourth-round match against 2022 champion Elena Rybakina. No. 10 Grigor Dimitrov retired from his fourth-round match against Daniil Medvedev with a leg problem.\n\n\"It's normal for the second week at Wimbledon to be feeling niggling things on your muscles, because it's tough -- the grass, getting down low, coming into the net. It's more on the muscles than the joints on the grass,\" 2003 Wimbledon finalist Mark Philippoussis said. \"So I'm sure lots of people will be feeling it now with little things here and there.\"\n\nCount Djokovic among those who think part of the issue is that all of the rain during the tournament -- so much that the mixed doubles final was shifted from Thursday to Sunday, and play began a half-hour earlier than usual on most courts Wednesday -- has made the grass more slick and the footing less sure.\n\nSo has shutting the retractable roofs at Centre Court and No. 1 Court, the only two arenas with that luxury during wet weather.\n\n\"Once you close the roof, you know that the grass is going to be more slippery. So there's more chances that players will fall. Unfortunately, some of the falls have caused some of the players to withdraw,\" Djokovic said.\n\n\"It's part of this surface. You can't really change that,\" added the seven-time champion at the All England Club. \"I mean, it's grass. It's a live surface, and it reacts to different conditions.\"\n\nThe pattern began at grass tourneys that preceded Wimbledon.\n\nMarketa Vondrousova retired from a match in Berlin after hurting her right leg there. When she showed up at Wimbledon, she became the first defending champion in 30 years to lose in the first round and acknowledged: \"I was a bit scared because of my leg.\"\n\nThe woman who beat her last week, Jessica Bouzas Maneiro, stopped at Wimbledon because of a back issue in the third round against Krejcikova.\n\nFrances Tiafoe pulled out of the Queen's Club tournament before Wimbledon after spraining a ligament in his right knee when he took a tumble. Tiafoe played at the All England Club with a black sleeve over his knee and made it to the third round before losing to defending champion Carlos Alcaraz.\n\nDjokovic tore the meniscus in his right knee during a match at the French Open, had surgery and returned to competition less than a month later. Ironically, he thinks it's possible that might have helped him stay upright this fortnight.\n\nThat's because, years ago, Djokovic was one of the first players to regularly slide on grass the way they do on clay. He has cut down on those movements this time at Wimbledon, being extra careful to avoid risking falls.\n\n\"It's probably part of my, I guess, different kind of movement on the court that I've been really experimenting with because of the cautiousness -- because of the knee and everything that was happening prior to the tournament,\" Djokovic explained. \"The first couple rounds, I was still not maybe willing to go (for difficult) balls and slide and make splits.\"\n\nOther theories include: More and more baseline play on grass, and less serving-and-volleying, creates longer points and extra running, which translate into a greater likelihood of slips; less comfort on grass because players tend to grow up practicing and competing on clay or hard courts; and a brief grass portion of the schedule that doesn't allow for accumulating a lot of experience on the turf.\n\nThen there's the general wear-and-tear of a season.\n\n\"Listen, tennis is a very physical sport at the moment. For sure, the rallies are longer. Matches. Scheduling. Finishing late,\" 2006 Australian Open finalist Marcos Baghdatis said. \"It's very demanding on the body. ... A lot of things are changing that (contribute) to players getting injured.\"\n\nCopyright 2024 The Associated Press. All rights reserved. Ironically, he thinks it's possible that might have helped him stay upright this fortnight.\n\nThat's because, years ago, Djokovic was one of the first players to regularly slide on grass the way they do on clay. He has cut down on those movements this time at Wimbledon, being extra careful to avoid risking falls.\n\n\"It's probably part of my, I guess, different kind of movement on the court that I've been really experimenting with because of the cautiousness -- because of the knee and everything that was happening prior to the tournament,\" Djokovic explained. \"The first couple rounds, I was still not maybe willing to go (for difficult) balls and slide and make splits.\"\n\nOther theories include: More and more baseline play on grass, and less serving-and-volleying, creates longer points and extra running, which translate into a greater likelihood of slips; less comfort on grass because players tend to grow up practicing and competing on clay or hard courts; and a brief grass portion of the schedule that doesn't allow for accumulating a lot of experience on the turf.\n\nThen there's the general wear-and-tear of a season.\n\n\"Listen, tennis is a very physical sport at the moment. {"uri": "2024-07-417749055", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "17:24:03", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T17:24:03Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T17:06:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8352941274642944, "url": "https://www.stltoday.com/wimbledon-injuries-djokovic-de-minaur-collins-keys/article_928d2c59-5a2f-5d30-87aa-8f1cb61b3dd0.html", "title": "Injuries are adding up at Wimbledon and determining the outcomes of matches", "body": "Novak Djokovic of Serbia falls during Monday's fourth round match against Holger Rune of Denmark at Wimbledon in London.\n\nHOWARD FENDRICH and KEN MAGUIRE Associated Press\n\nLONDON -- There's no single explanation, of course, for all of the injuries to players in the latter stages at Wimbledon this year. This much is certain: The timing could hardly be worse.\n\nThe man Novak Djokovic was supposed to face on Wednesday, Alex de Minaur, withdrew hours before their scheduled quarterfinal because he jarred his hip at the end of a victory two days earlier.\n\n\"I'm devastated,\" de Minaur said. \"The problem with me going out and playing is that one stretch, one slide, one anything, can make this injury (recovery) go from three to six weeks to four months. It's too much to risk.\"\n\nTaylor Fritz's fourth-round opponent, Alexander Zverev, slipped on an unworn patch of green grass in his previous match. That caused a bone bruise -- and maybe worse -- that Zverev complained left him on \"one leg\" in his loss to the American at what the two-time major finalist characterized as a wide open opportunity to grab a first Grand Slam title.\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\nPi Pizzeria closing last remaining location, in St. Louis' Central West End McClellan: Downtown St. Louis is a casualty of the do-gooder war Tropical Storm Beryl could hit St. Louis area with 4 inches of rain or more Hulk Hogan starts selling his new Real American Beer in Missouri, competing with Bud Light BenFred: Why Jordan Walker's name will provide hot-stove fodder as Cardinals near trade deadline Wentzville schools investigated board members for alleged retaliation, ethics violations Parades, fireworks and parties: Your guide to July 4th in St. Louis Several St. Louis-area cities move fireworks shows to Friday. Others cancel events. Messenger: She sold her house to help support her baby. Missouri's AG wants the money. Two dead in North County, 7 injured in downtown St. Louis during Fourth of July shootings Lars Nootbaar wondered about his spate of injuries, too. So he sought answers: Cardinals Extra July 4 chaos reignites safety concerns in downtown St. Louis. Chief blames 'small' group. Messenger: Judge fines St. Louis attorney for hiding documents on police shootings Extra! Extra! Read all about Cardinals' comeback from 5-run deficit to outlast Nats in 11th BenFred: Eli Drinkwitz's relentless recruiting has Mizzou beating brand names in away games\n\nDanielle Collins' last Wimbledon appearance before retirement ended with tape wrapped around her hamstring, the work of a trainer during the American's fourth-round loss to 2021 French Open champion Barbora Krejcikova. Collins chalked it up to failing to \"think about every little step that you take.\"\n\n\"There's been a ton of injuries on the grass. Left and right, it seems like people are going down. I am, I guess, frustrated that I feel like I was focusing on my tactics and kind of what I needed to do to play at a high level. Usually, I feel like, on other surfaces you're not having to think so critically about your movement,\" Collins said. \"The one second I take my mind off of it, not think about every little thing I'm doing with my footwork, it ends up happening.\"\n\nThe falls keep happening. The injuries are adding up.\n\n\"It's unfortunate, obviously,\" de Minaur said. \"You never want to see this.\"\n\nHe called his mishap \"more of a freak injury,\" related to the \"excessive amount of force\" used to slide on grass.\n\nMadison Keys, the 2017 U.S. Open runner-up, was in tears when she stopped because of a hurt leg at 5-all in the third set of a Week 2 match against Jasmine Paolini, who reached Thursday's semifinals.\n\nEmma Raducanu, who won the U.S. Open three years ago, withdrew from mixed doubles -- which was supposed to be Andy Murray's last event at Wimbledon -- because of a sore wrist, then needed a medical timeout later that day after falling in the third set of a singles loss.\n\nNo. 17 Anna Kalinskaya cited a bad wrist when she quit in her fourth-round match against 2022 champion Elena Rybakina. No. 10 Grigor Dimitrov retired from his fourth-round match against Daniil Medvedev with a leg problem.\n\n\"It's normal for the second week at Wimbledon to be feeling niggling things on your muscles, because it's tough -- the grass, getting down low, coming into the net. It's more on the muscles than the joints on the grass,\" 2003 Wimbledon finalist Mark Philippoussis said. \"So I'm sure lots of people will be feeling it now with little things here and there.\"\n\nCount Djokovic among those who think part of the issue is that all of the rain during the tournament -- so much that the mixed doubles final was shifted from Thursday to Sunday, and play began a half-hour earlier than usual on most courts Wednesday -- has made the grass more slick and the footing less sure.\n\nSo has shutting the retractable roofs at Centre Court and No. 1 Court, the only two arenas with that luxury during wet weather.\n\n\"Once you close the roof, you know that the grass is going to be more slippery. So there's more chances that players will fall. Unfortunately, some of the falls have caused some of the players to withdraw,\" Djokovic said.\n\n\"It's part of this surface. You can't really change that,\" added the seven-time champion at the All England Club. \"I mean, it's grass. It's a live surface, and it reacts to different conditions.\"\n\nThe pattern began at grass tourneys that preceded Wimbledon.\n\nMarketa Vondrousova retired from a match in Berlin after hurting her right leg there. When she showed up at Wimbledon, she became the first defending champion in 30 years to lose in the first round and acknowledged: \"I was a bit scared because of my leg.\"\n\nThe woman who beat her last week, Jessica Bouzas Maneiro, stopped at Wimbledon because of a back issue in the third round against Krejcikova.\n\nFrances Tiafoe pulled out of the Queen's Club tournament before Wimbledon after spraining a ligament in his right knee when he took a tumble. Tiafoe played at the All England Club with a black sleeve over his knee and made it to the third round before losing to defending champion Carlos Alcaraz.\n\nDjokovic tore the meniscus in his right knee during a match at the French Open, had surgery and returned to competition less than a month later. Ironically, he thinks it's possible that might have helped him stay upright this fortnight.\n\nThat's because, years ago, Djokovic was one of the first players to regularly slide on grass the way they do on clay. He has cut down on those movements this time at Wimbledon, being extra careful to avoid risking falls.\n\n\"It's probably part of my, I guess, different kind of movement on the court that I've been really experimenting with because of the cautiousness -- because of the knee and everything that was happening prior to the tournament,\" Djokovic explained. \"The first couple rounds, I was still not maybe willing to go (for difficult) balls and slide and make splits.\"\n\nOther theories include: More and more baseline play on grass, and less serving-and-volleying, creates longer points and extra running, which translate into a greater likelihood of slips; less comfort on grass because players tend to grow up practicing and competing on clay or hard courts; and a brief grass portion of the schedule that doesn't allow for accumulating a lot of experience on the turf.\n\nThen there's the general wear-and-tear of a season.\n\n\"Listen, tennis is a very physical sport at the moment. For sure, the rallies are longer. Matches. Scheduling. Finishing late,\" 2006 Australian Open finalist Marcos Baghdatis said. \"It's very demanding on the body. ... A lot of things are changing that (contribute) to players getting injured.\"\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your inbox!\n\nSign up!\n\n* I understand and agree that I am, I guess, frustrated that I feel like I was focusing on my tactics and kind of what I needed to do to play at a high level. Usually, I feel like, on other surfaces you're not having to think so critically about your movement,\" Collins said. \"The one second I take my mind off of it, not think about every little thing I'm doing with my footwork, it ends up happening.\"\n\nThe falls keep happening. The injuries are adding up.\n\n\"It's unfortunate, obviously,\" de Minaur said. \"You never want to see this.\"\n\nHe called his mishap \"more of a freak injury,\" related to the \"excessive amount of force\" used to slide on grass.\n\nMadison Keys, the 2017 U.S. Open runner-up, was in tears when she stopped because of a hurt leg at 5-all in the third set of a Week 2 match against Jasmine Paolini, who reached Thursday's semifinals.\n\nEmma Raducanu, who won the U.S. Open three years ago, withdrew from mixed doubles -- which was supposed to be Andy Murray's last event at Wimbledon -- because of a sore wrist, then needed a medical timeout later that day after falling in the third set of a singles loss.\n\nNo. 17 Anna Kalinskaya cited a bad wrist when she quit in her fourth-round match against 2022 champion Elena Rybakina. No. 10 Grigor Dimitrov retired from his fourth-round match against Daniil Medvedev with a leg problem.\n\n\"It's normal for the second week at Wimbledon to be feeling niggling things on your muscles, because it's tough -- the grass, getting down low, coming into the net. It's more on the muscles than the joints on the grass,\" 2003 Wimbledon finalist Mark Philippoussis said. \"So I'm sure lots of people will be feeling it now with little things here and there.\"\n\nCount Djokovic among those who think part of the issue is that all of the rain during the tournament -- so much that the mixed doubles final was shifted from Thursday to Sunday, and play began a half-hour earlier than usual on most courts Wednesday -- has made the grass more slick and the footing less sure.\n\nSo has shutting the retractable roofs at Centre Court and No. 1 Court, the only two arenas with that luxury during wet weather.\n\n\"Once you close the roof, you know that the grass is going to be more slippery. So there's more chances that players will fall. Unfortunately, some of the falls have caused some of the players to withdraw,\" Djokovic said.\n\n\"It's part of this surface. You can't really change that,\" added the seven-time champion at the All England Club. \"I mean, it's grass. It's a live surface, and it reacts to different conditions.\"\n\nThe pattern began at grass tourneys that preceded Wimbledon.\n\nMarketa Vondrousova retired from a match in Berlin after hurting her right leg there. When she showed up at Wimbledon, she became the first defending champion in 30 years to lose in the first round and acknowledged: \"I was a bit scared because of my leg.\"\n\nThe woman who beat her last week, Jessica Bouzas Maneiro, stopped at Wimbledon because of a back issue in the third round against Krejcikova.\n\nFrances Tiafoe pulled out of the Queen's Club tournament before Wimbledon after spraining a ligament in his right knee when he took a tumble. Tiafoe played at the All England Club with a black sleeve over his knee and made it to the third round before losing to defending champion Carlos Alcaraz.\n\nDjokovic tore the meniscus in his right knee during a match at the French Open, had surgery and returned to competition less than a month later. Ironically, he thinks it's possible that might have helped him stay upright this fortnight.\n\nThat's because, years ago, Djokovic was one of the first players to regularly slide on grass the way they do on clay. He has cut down on those movements this time at Wimbledon, being extra careful to avoid risking falls.\n\n\"It's probably part of my, I guess, different kind of movement on the court that I've been really experimenting with because of the cautiousness -- because of the knee and everything that was happening prior to the tournament,\" Djokovic explained. \"The first couple rounds, I was still not maybe willing to go (for difficult) balls and slide and make splits.\"\n\nOther theories include: More and more baseline play on grass, and less serving-and-volleying, creates longer points and extra running, which translate into a greater likelihood of slips; less comfort on grass because players tend to grow up practicing and competing on clay or hard courts; and a brief grass portion of the schedule that doesn't allow for accumulating a lot of experience on the turf.\n\nThen there's the general wear-and-tear of a season.\n\n\"Listen, tennis is a very physical sport at the moment. For sure, the rallies are longer. Matches. Scheduling. Finishing late,\" 2006 Australian Open finalist Marcos Baghdatis said. \"It's very demanding on the body. ... A lot of things are changing that (contribute) to players getting injured.\"\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your inbox!\n\nSign up! * I understand and agree that registration This much is certain: The timing could hardly be worse.\n\nThe man Novak Djokovic was supposed to face on Wednesday, Alex de Minaur, withdrew hours before their scheduled quarterfinal because he jarred his hip at the end of a victory two days earlier.\n\n\"I'm devastated,\" de Minaur said. \"The problem with me going out and playing is that one stretch, one slide, one anything, can make this injury (recovery) go from three to six weeks to four months. It's too much to risk.\"\n\nTaylor Fritz's fourth-round opponent, Alexander Zverev, slipped on an unworn patch of green grass in his previous match. That caused a bone bruise -- and maybe worse -- that Zverev complained left him on \"one leg\" in his loss to the American at what the two-time major finalist characterized as a wide open opportunity to grab a first Grand Slam title.\n\nPeople are also reading... 3 dead in plane crash near McDowell, Rutherford county line Plane crashes in McDowell; emergency crews ask people to avoid Brackett Town Road Marion man meets McDowell paramedic who saved his life 24 years ago Toddler killed by truck at Steel Creek campground north of Morganton Golden Hour Charcuterie opens in Marion's Depot District 3 from South Carolina die in Nebo plane crash; national safety board investigating Body found Sunday was Marion man; cause of death pending toxicology report Nebo woman charged with break-in McDowell Little League crowns two champions; league to host a baseball state tournament Burkemont Mountain in Morganton ablaze with 30-acre wildfire When are the 4th of July fireworks, parades in McDowell County? McDowell calendar: Independence Day parades set for today Mission Hospital McDowell's new CEO planning to get to know the community Marion's Spillway Bridge & Co. set to reopen under new owners Marion youth festival to celebrate Latinx culture, eliminating language barriers\n\nDanielle Collins' last Wimbledon appearance before retirement ended with tape wrapped around her hamstring, the work of a trainer during the American's fourth-round loss to 2021 French Open champion Barbora Krejcikova. Collins chalked it up to failing to \"think about every little step that you take.\"\n\n\"There's been a ton of injuries on the grass. Left and right, it seems like people are going down. I am, I guess, frustrated that I feel like I was focusing on my tactics and kind of what I needed to do to play at a high level. Usually, I feel like, on other surfaces you're not having to think so critically about your movement,\" Collins said. \"The one second I take my mind off of it, not think about every little thing I'm doing with my footwork, it ends up happening.\"\n\nThe falls keep happening. The injuries are adding up.\n\n\"It's unfortunate, obviously,\" de Minaur said. \"You never want to see this.\"\n\nHe called his mishap \"more of a freak injury,\" related to the \"excessive amount of force\" used to slide on grass.\n\nMadison Keys, the 2017 U.S. Open runner-up, was in tears when she stopped because of a hurt leg at 5-all in the third set of a Week 2 match against Jasmine Paolini, who reached Thursday's semifinals.\n\nEmma Raducanu, who won the U.S. Open three years ago, withdrew from mixed doubles -- which was supposed to be Andy Murray's last event at Wimbledon -- because of a sore wrist, then needed a medical timeout later that day after falling in the third set of a singles loss.\n\nNo. 17 Anna Kalinskaya cited a bad wrist when she quit in her fourth-round match against 2022 champion Elena Rybakina. No. 10 Grigor Dimitrov retired from his fourth-round match against Daniil Medvedev with a leg problem.\n\n\"It's normal for the second week at Wimbledon to be feeling niggling things on your muscles, because it's tough -- the grass, getting down low, coming into the net. It's more on the muscles than the joints on the grass,\" 2003 Wimbledon finalist Mark Philippoussis said. \"So I'm sure lots of people will be feeling it now with little things here and there.\"\n\nCount Djokovic among those who think part of the issue is that all of the rain during the tournament -- so much that the mixed doubles final was shifted from Thursday to Sunday, and play began a half-hour earlier than usual on most courts Wednesday -- has made the grass more slick and the footing less sure.\n\nSo has shutting the retractable roofs at Centre Court and No. 1 Court, the only two arenas with that luxury during wet weather.\n\n\"Once you close the roof, you know that the grass is going to be more slippery. So there's more chances that players will fall. Unfortunately, some of the falls have caused some of the players to withdraw,\" Djokovic said.\n\n\"It's part of this surface. You can't really change that,\" added the seven-time champion at the All England Club. \"I mean, it's grass. It's a live surface, and it reacts to different conditions.\"\n\nThe pattern began at grass tourneys that preceded Wimbledon.\n\nMarketa Vondrousova retired from a match in Berlin after hurting her right leg there. When she showed up at Wimbledon, she became the first defending champion in 30 years to lose in the first round and acknowledged: \"I was a bit scared because of my leg.\"\n\nThe woman who beat her last week, Jessica Bouzas Maneiro, stopped at Wimbledon because of a back issue in the third round against Krejcikova.\n\nFrances Tiafoe pulled out of the Queen's Club tournament before Wimbledon after spraining a ligament in his right knee when he took a tumble. Tiafoe played at the All England Club with a black sleeve over his knee and made it to the third round before losing to defending champion Carlos Alcaraz.\n\nDjokovic tore the meniscus in his right knee during a match at the French Open, had surgery and returned to competition less than a month later. Ironically, he thinks it's possible that might have helped him stay upright this fortnight.\n\nThat's because, years ago, Djokovic was one of the first players to regularly slide on grass the way they do on clay. He has cut down on those movements this time at Wimbledon, being extra careful to avoid risking falls.\n\n\"It's probably part of my, I guess, different kind of movement on the court that I've been really experimenting with because of the cautiousness -- because of the knee and everything that was happening prior to the tournament,\" Djokovic explained. \"The first couple rounds, I was still not maybe willing to go (for difficult) balls and slide and make splits.\"\n\nOther theories include: More and more baseline play on grass, and less serving-and-volleying, creates longer points and extra running, which translate into a greater likelihood of slips; less comfort on grass because players tend to grow up practicing and competing on clay or hard courts; and a brief grass portion of the schedule that doesn't allow for accumulating a lot of experience on the turf.\n\nThen there's the general wear-and-tear of a season.\n\n\"Listen, tennis is a very physical sport at the moment. For sure, the rallies are longer. Matches. Scheduling. Finishing late,\" 2006 Australian Open finalist Marcos Baghdatis said. \"It's very demanding on the body. ... {"uri": "8219145510", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "17:29:02", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T17:29:02Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T17:28:05Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.8196078538894653, "url": "https://lacrossetribune.com/wimbledon-injuries-djokovic-de-minaur-collins-keys/article_f5f092de-6eef-54d4-b42c-b2b44f52b242.html", "title": "Injuries are adding up at Wimbledon and determining the outcomes of matches", "body": "Novak Djokovic of Serbia falls during Monday's fourth round match against Holger Rune of Denmark at Wimbledon in London.\n\nHOWARD FENDRICH and KEN MAGUIRE Associated Press\n\nLONDON -- There's no single explanation, of course, for all of the injuries to players in the latter stages at Wimbledon this year. This much is certain: The timing could hardly be worse.\n\nThe man Novak Djokovic was supposed to face on Wednesday, Alex de Minaur, withdrew hours before their scheduled quarterfinal because he jarred his hip at the end of a victory two days earlier.\n\n\"I'm devastated,\" de Minaur said. \"The problem with me going out and playing is that one stretch, one slide, one anything, can make this injury (recovery) go from three to six weeks to four months. It's too much to risk.\"\n\nTaylor Fritz's fourth-round opponent, Alexander Zverev, slipped on an unworn patch of green grass in his previous match. That caused a bone bruise -- and maybe worse -- that Zverev complained left him on \"one leg\" in his loss to the American at what the two-time major finalist characterized as a wide open opportunity to grab a first Grand Slam title.\n\nPeople are also reading... Steve Cahalan: Batter and Brew hits the streets in La Crosse Steve Cahalan: Downtown Holiday Inn converting to boutique hotel in La Crosse Steve Cahalan: Crumbl Cookies to begin construction in late summer in Onalaska Skyrockers firework show postponed; more closures due to rain and flooding Local talent shines in first-ever Riverfest Idol karaoke tournament Dane County judge strikes down part of Act 10 One injured in three vehicle crash near Arcadia Viroqua's fireworks show postponed due to inclement weather Galesville man charged with eluding police and stealing vehicle in Onalaska Onalaska man charged with felony for pointing gun at woman's head during argument Two spills of chemical firefighting foam occurred at La Crosse Regional Airport earlier this year, reports say Vernon County gears up for Independence Day celebrations Alaska man faces felony charges for assault in Winona that led to shelter-in-place order Tellurian awarded $35,000 from Mayo Health Spring boys all-conference teams: Complete Coulee Region listing\n\nDanielle Collins' last Wimbledon appearance before retirement ended with tape wrapped around her hamstring, the work of a trainer during the American's fourth-round loss to 2021 French Open champion Barbora Krejcikova. Collins chalked it up to failing to \"think about every little step that you take.\"\n\n\"There's been a ton of injuries on the grass. Left and right, it seems like people are going down. I am, I guess, frustrated that I feel like I was focusing on my tactics and kind of what I needed to do to play at a high level. Usually, I feel like, on other surfaces you're not having to think so critically about your movement,\" Collins said. \"The one second I take my mind off of it, not think about every little thing I'm doing with my footwork, it ends up happening.\"\n\nThe falls keep happening. The injuries are adding up.\n\n\"It's unfortunate, obviously,\" de Minaur said. \"You never want to see this.\"\n\nHe called his mishap \"more of a freak injury,\" related to the \"excessive amount of force\" used to slide on grass.\n\nMadison Keys, the 2017 U.S. Open runner-up, was in tears when she stopped because of a hurt leg at 5-all in the third set of a Week 2 match against Jasmine Paolini, who reached Thursday's semifinals.\n\nEmma Raducanu, who won the U.S. Open three years ago, withdrew from mixed doubles -- which was supposed to be Andy Murray's last event at Wimbledon -- because of a sore wrist, then needed a medical timeout later that day after falling in the third set of a singles loss.\n\nNo. 17 Anna Kalinskaya cited a bad wrist when she quit in her fourth-round match against 2022 champion Elena Rybakina. No. 10 Grigor Dimitrov retired from his fourth-round match against Daniil Medvedev with a leg problem.\n\n\"It's normal for the second week at Wimbledon to be feeling niggling things on your muscles, because it's tough -- the grass, getting down low, coming into the net. It's more on the muscles than the joints on the grass,\" 2003 Wimbledon finalist Mark Philippoussis said. \"So I'm sure lots of people will be feeling it now with little things here and there.\"\n\nCount Djokovic among those who think part of the issue is that all of the rain during the tournament -- so much that the mixed doubles final was shifted from Thursday to Sunday, and play began a half-hour earlier than usual on most courts Wednesday -- has made the grass more slick and the footing less sure.\n\nSo has shutting the retractable roofs at Centre Court and No. 1 Court, the only two arenas with that luxury during wet weather.\n\n\"Once you close the roof, you know that the grass is going to be more slippery. So there's more chances that players will fall. Unfortunately, some of the falls have caused some of the players to withdraw,\" Djokovic said.\n\n\"It's part of this surface. You can't really change that,\" added the seven-time champion at the All England Club. \"I mean, it's grass. It's a live surface, and it reacts to different conditions.\"\n\nThe pattern began at grass tourneys that preceded Wimbledon.\n\nMarketa Vondrousova retired from a match in Berlin after hurting her right leg there. When she showed up at Wimbledon, she became the first defending champion in 30 years to lose in the first round and acknowledged: \"I was a bit scared because of my leg.\"\n\nThe woman who beat her last week, Jessica Bouzas Maneiro, stopped at Wimbledon because of a back issue in the third round against Krejcikova.\n\nFrances Tiafoe pulled out of the Queen's Club tournament before Wimbledon after spraining a ligament in his right knee when he took a tumble. Tiafoe played at the All England Club with a black sleeve over his knee and made it to the third round before losing to defending champion Carlos Alcaraz.\n\nDjokovic tore the meniscus in his right knee during a match at the French Open, had surgery and returned to competition less than a month later. Ironically, he thinks it's possible that might have helped him stay upright this fortnight.\n\nThat's because, years ago, Djokovic was one of the first players to regularly slide on grass the way they do on clay. He has cut down on those movements this time at Wimbledon, being extra careful to avoid risking falls.\n\n\"It's probably part of my, I guess, different kind of movement on the court that I've been really experimenting with because of the cautiousness -- because of the knee and everything that was happening prior to the tournament,\" Djokovic explained. \"The first couple rounds, I was still not maybe willing to go (for difficult) balls and slide and make splits.\"\n\nOther theories include: More and more baseline play on grass, and less serving-and-volleying, creates longer points and extra running, which translate into a greater likelihood of slips; less comfort on grass because players tend to grow up practicing and competing on clay or hard courts; and a brief grass portion of the schedule that doesn't allow for accumulating a lot of experience on the turf.\n\nThen there's the general wear-and-tear of a season.\n\n\"Listen, tennis is a very physical sport at the moment. For sure, the rallies are longer. Matches. Scheduling. Finishing late,\" 2006 Australian Open finalist Marcos Baghdatis said. \"It's very demanding on the body. ... A lot of things are changing that (contribute) to players getting injured.\"\n\nLove 0 Funny 0 Wow 0 Sad 0 Angry 0\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your inbox!\n\nSign up! * I You can't really change that,\" added the seven-time champion at the All England Club. \"I mean, it's grass. It's a live surface, and it reacts to different conditions.\"\n\nThe pattern began at grass tourneys that preceded Wimbledon.\n\nMarketa Vondrousova retired from a match in Berlin after hurting her right leg there. Ironically, he thinks it's possible that might have helped him stay upright this fortnight.\n\nThat's because, years ago, Djokovic was one of the first players to regularly slide on grass the way they do on clay. He has cut down on those movements this time at Wimbledon, being extra careful to avoid risking falls.\n\n\"It's probably part of my, I guess, different kind of movement on the court that I've been really experimenting with because of the cautiousness -- because of the knee and everything that was happening prior to the tournament,\" Djokovic explained. \"The first couple rounds, I was still not maybe willing to go (for difficult) balls and slide and make splits.\"\n\nOther theories include: More and more baseline play on grass, and less serving-and-volleying, creates longer points and extra running, which translate into a greater likelihood of slips; less comfort on grass because players tend to grow up practicing and competing on clay or hard courts; and a brief grass portion of the schedule that doesn't allow for accumulating a lot of experience on the turf.\n\nThen there's the general wear-and-tear of a season.\n\n\"Listen, tennis is a very physical sport at the moment. For sure, the rallies are longer. Matches. Scheduling. Finishing late,\" 2006 Australian Open finalist Marcos Baghdatis said. \"It's very demanding on the body. ... {"uri": "8218857024", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-10", "time": "14:10:53", "dateTime": "2024-07-10T14:10:53Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-10T14:09:19Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.7647058963775635, "url": "https://www.dailymail.co.uk/wires/ap/article-13620153/Injures-adding-Wimbledon-determining-outcomes-matches.html", "title": "Injures are adding up at Wimbledon and determining the outcomes of...", "body": "LONDON (AP) - There's no single explanation, of course, for all of the injuries to players in the latter stages at Wimbledon this year. This much is certain: The timing could hardly be worse.\n\nThe man Novak Djokovic was supposed to face on Wednesday, Alex de Minaur, withdrew hours before their scheduled quarterfinal because he jarred his hip at the end of a victory two days earlier.\n\n\"I'm devastated,\" de Minaur said. \"The problem with me going out and playing is that one stretch, one slide, one anything, can make this injury (recovery) go from three to six weeks to four months. It\u00b4s too much to risk.\"\n\nTaylor Fritz\u00b4s fourth-round opponent, Alexander Zverev, slipped on an unworn patch of green grass in his previous match. That caused a bone bruise - and maybe worse - that Zverev complained left him on \"one leg\" in his loss to the American at what the two-time major finalist characterized as a wide open opportunity to grab a first Grand Slam title.\n\nDanielle Collins' last Wimbledon appearance before retirement ended with tape wrapped around her hamstring, the work of a trainer during the American\u00b4s fourth-round loss to 2021 French Open champion Barbora Krejcikova. Collins chalked it up to failing to \"think about every little step that you take.\"\n\n\"There's been a ton of injuries on the grass. Left and right, it seems like people are going down. I am, I guess, frustrated that I feel like I was focusing on my tactics and kind of what I needed to do to play at a high level. Usually, I feel like, on other surfaces you\u00b4re not having to think so critically about your movement,\" Collins said. \"The one second I take my mind off of it, not think about every little thing I\u00b4m doing with my footwork, it ends up happening.\"\n\nAlex de Minaur of Australia falls during his fourth round match against Arthur Fils of France at the Wimbledon tennis championships in London, Monday, July 8, 2024. (AP Photo/Alberto Pezzali)\n\nThe falls keep happening. The injuries are adding up.\n\n\"It\u00b4s unfortunate, obviously,\" de Minaur said. \"You never want to see this.\"\n\nHe called his mishap \"more of a freak injury,\" related to the \"excessive amount of force\" used to slide on grass.\n\nMadison Keys, the 2017 U.S. Open runner-up, was in tears when she stopped because of a hurt leg at 5-all in the third set of a Week 2 match against Jasmine Paolini, who reached Thursday's semifinals.\n\nEmma Raducanu, who won the U.S. Open three years ago, withdrew from mixed doubles - which was supposed to be Andy Murray's last event at Wimbledon - because of a sore wrist, then needed a medical timeout later that day after falling in the third set of a singles loss.\n\nNo. 17 Anna Kalinskaya cited a bad wrist when she quit in her fourth-round match against 2022 champion Elena Rybakina. No. 10 Grigor Dimitrov retired from his fourth-round match against Daniil Medvedev with a leg problem.\n\n\"It\u00b4s normal for the second week at Wimbledon to be feeling niggling things on your muscles, because it\u00b4s tough - the grass, getting down low, coming into the net. It's more on the muscles than the joints on the grass,\" 2003 Wimbledon finalist Mark Philippoussis said. \"So I\u00b4m sure lots of people will be feeling it now with little things here and there.\"\n\nCount Djokovic among those who think part of the issue is that all of the rain during the tournament - so much that the mixed doubles final was shifted from Thursday to Sunday, and play began a half-hour earlier than usual on most courts Wednesday - has made the grass more slick and the footing less sure.\n\nSo has shutting the retractable roofs at Centre Court and No. 1 Court, the only two arenas with that luxury during wet weather.\n\n\"Once you close the roof, you know that the grass is going to be more slippery. So there\u00b4s more chances that players will fall. Unfortunately, some of the falls have caused some of the players to withdraw,\" Djokovic said.\n\n\"It's part of this surface. You can\u00b4t really change that,\" added the seven-time champion at the All England Club. \"I mean, it\u00b4s grass. It\u00b4s a live surface, and it reacts to different conditions.\"\n\nThe pattern began at grass tourneys that preceded Wimbledon.\n\nMarketa Vondrousova retired from a match in Berlin after hurting her right leg there. When she showed up at Wimbledon, she became the first defending champion in 30 years to lose in the first round and acknowledged: \"I was a bit scared because of my leg.\"\n\nThe woman who beat her last week, Jessica Bouzas Maneiro, stopped at Wimbledon because of a back issue in the third round against Krejcikova.\n\nFrances Tiafoe pulled out of the Queen's Club tournament before Wimbledon after spraining a ligament in his right knee when he took a tumble. Tiafoe played at the All England Club with a black sleeve over his knee and made it to the third round before losing to defending champion Carlos Alcaraz.\n\nDjokovic tore the meniscus in his right knee during a match at the French Open, had surgery and returned to competition less than a month later. Ironically, he thinks it's possible that might have helped him stay upright this fortnight.\n\nThat's because, years ago, Djokovic was one of the first players to regularly slide on grass the way they do on clay. He has cut down on those movements this time at Wimbledon, being extra careful to avoid risking falls.\n\n\"It\u00b4s probably part of my, I guess, different kind of movement on the court that I\u00b4ve been really experimenting with because of the cautiousness - because of the knee and everything that was happening prior to the tournament,\" Djokovic explained. \"The first couple rounds, I was still not maybe willing to go (for difficult) balls and slide and make splits.\"\n\nOther theories include: More and more baseline play on grass, and less serving-and-volleying, creates longer points and extra running, which translate into a greater likelihood of slips; less comfort on grass because players tend to grow up practicing and competing on clay or hard courts; and a brief grass portion of the schedule that doesn't allow for accumulating a lot of experience on the turf.\n\nThen there's the general wear-and-tear of a season.\n\n\"Listen, tennis is a very physical sport at the moment. For sure, the rallies are longer. Matches. Scheduling. Finishing late,\" 2006 Australian Open finalist Marcos Baghdatis said. \"It\u00b4s very demanding on the body. ... A lot of things are changing that (contribute) to players getting injured.\"\n\nAP tennis: https://apnews.com/hub/tennis\n\nNovak Djokovic of Serbia falls during his fourth round match against Holger Rune of Denmark at the Wimbledon tennis championships in London, Monday, July 8, 2024. (AP Photo/Mosa'ab Elshamy)\n\nMadison Keys of the United States reacts during her fourth round match against Jasmine Paolini of Italy at the Wimbledon tennis championships in London, Sunday, July 7, 2024. (AP Photo/Kirsty Wigglesworth)\n\nSpain's Carlos Alcaraz, left, looks over at Francis Tiafoe of the United States who fell during their third round match at the Wimbledon tennis championships in London, Friday, July 5, 2024. (AP Photo/Alberto Pezzali)\n\nDanielle Collins plays a forehand return to Barbora Krejcikova of the Czech Republic during their fourth round match at the Wimbledon tennis championships in London, Monday, July 8, 2024. (AP Photo/Alberto Pezzali)\n\nLorenzo Musetti of Italy falls to the ground as he celebrates after defeating Giovanni Mpetshi Perricard of France in their fourth round match at the Wimbledon tennis championships in London, Monday, July 8, 2024. (AP Photo/Mosa'ab Elshamy)\n\nCarlos Alcaraz of Spain falls during his fourth round match against Ugo Humbert of France at the Wimbledon tennis championships in London, Sunday, July 7, 2024. Play on No 1 Court began at 1pm while Centre was 1.30pm. The remaining 16 courts saw outdoor play between the hours of 11am and 6pm before retiring for the evening a few hours early due to the inclement weather.\n\nDjokovic's fourth-round victory over Hune took place during the showers - which continued into Tuesday at the All England Club - yet in the aftermath of victory, the Serb highlighted the high incidence of injuries and the correlation with roof closures generally at the Championships.\n\n\"Once you close the roof, it feels like we've been playing an indoor grass court tournament here more than outdoor, to be honest,\" Djokovic said. \"I played every single match basically indoors. We are blessed to have the roof, don't get me wrong, particularly on the two big courts. I feel the frustration of the players obviously because you get interrupted many times during the day. Obviously it's not something you can affect. The rain is there, it's there.\n\n\"But once you close the roof, you know that the grass is going to be more slippery. So there's more chances that players will fall. Unfortunately some of the falls have caused some of the players to withdraw, like Dimitrov yesterday. We saw Zverev as well in his previous match slightly injure his knee.\n\n\"It's part of this surface. You can't really change that. I mean, it's grass. It's a live surface, and it reacts to different conditions, the humidity etc.\"\n\nAlthough SW19 is an outdoor event, Wimbledon installed a roof on Centre Court, which takes 10 minutes to retract, in 2009 with No 1 Court's own following 10 years later to mitigate for the volatile British summer, which has plagued this year's championship.\n\nProtocol dictates that the match referees, with oversight from the tournament director, have ultimate control over the use of the roofs and their decision is final. Telegraph Sport understands that officials erred on the side of caution on Monday due to the forecasted downpours. Should play start \"outdoors\" on the two main showcourts, the arrival of rain can cause delays with the covers having to be brought on, the players departing, and the roof closing. Then, under the new \"indoor\" conditions, players are entitled to a full warm-up before play recommences.\n\nOn Monday evening, Matt Roberts, host of the highly respected Tennis Podcast, speaking about Elena Rybakina's fourth-round win over Anna Kalinskaya agreed with Djokovic, branding the situation \"bizarre\" and \"odd\".\n\n\"It did not start raining here until 6pm, which is when play was suspended on the outdoor courts,\" Roberts said. \"There was no rain until then, play continued on the outdoor courts. Yet they played all matches on Centre and No 1 Court under the roof.\n\n\"It wasn't raining and we played indoors all day. It has just become accepted now. I understand anticipating rain and not wanting to change conditions mid-match, but I think that would be preferable to what we had today which was just an indoor tournament on the two main showcourts.\n\n\"The roofs are so quick to close now, and there isn't that much of a delay, it is just about the change of conditions, which is a factor that [the organisers] consider more important. I just think it is bizarre that it was perfectly dry here today and there could have been four-and-a-half or five hours of play on the show courts and we were indoors. I just think that is odd.\"\n\nWimbledon officials informed Telegraph Sport that the match referees, with oversight from the tournament director, have ultimate control over the use of the roofs and their decision is final.\n\nTelegraph Sport understands, however, that the referee does have the ability to request the roof shut for the start of a match if the forecast indicates that rain is imminent and that, on Monday, officials will have been swayed by the forecasted downpours. Should play start \"outdoors\" on the two main showcourts, the arrival of rain can cause delays with the covers having to be brought on, the players departing, and the roof closing.
